Recommended Actions After Using the Toilet
List of recommended actions and supplications after toilet

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two sunnah actions when leaving the toilet and recite the supplication taught by the Prophet (PBUH).

Core Idea
Leaving the toilet with the right foot and saying 'Ghufranak' transform a private moment into an act of worship.

This lesson focuses on the positive etiquettes of exiting the toilet. The source text prescribes stepping out with the right foot and uttering specific supplications that acknowledge Allah's blessings. These actions are sunnah and carry deep spiritual meaning, turning a mundane act into a moment of gratitude.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended actions
Read the passage and note the two actions: exiting with the right foot and saying the supplications.
2
Understand the reasoning
The right foot is preferred because it is associated with blessings and removing filth. The supplications express gratitude for health.
3
Memorize the supplications
The passage includes two supplications: 'Alhamdulillah...' and 'Ghufranak'. Learn their meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وذكر الله فلا يحمد إذا عطس، ولا يشمت عاطساً، ولا يرد سلاماً ولا مؤذناً ولا ينظر لعورته ولا إلى الخارج منها ولا يبصق ولا يتمخط ولا يتنحن ولا يكثر الإلتفات، ولا يعبث ببدنه ولا يرفع بصره إلى السماء ولا يطيل الجلوس لأنه يورث الباسور، ووجع الكبد ويخرج من الخلاء برجله اليمنى لأنها أحق بالتقدم لنعمة الإنصراف الأذى ومحل الشياطين (ثم يقول) بعد الخروج (الحمد الله الذي أذهب عني الأذى) بخروج الفضلات الممرضة بحبسها وعافاني) بإبقاء خاصية الغذاء الذي لو أمسك كله أو خرج لكان مظنة الهلاك، وقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عند خروجه غفرانك وهو كناية عن الإعتراف بالقصور عن بلوغ حق شكر نعمة الإطعام وتصريف خاصية الغذاء وتسهيل خروج الأذى
His saying: (And mention of Allah, so he does not praise if he sneezes, nor responds to a sneezer, nor returns a greeting, nor responds to the call to prayer, nor looks at his awrah nor at what comes out of him, nor spits, nor blows his nose, nor clears his throat, nor turns around much, nor fidgets with his body, nor raises his eyes to the sky, nor prolongs sitting because it causes hemorrhoids and liver pain, and he leaves the toilet with his right foot because it is more deserving of precedence for the blessing of leaving the filth and the dwelling of devils. (Then he says) after leaving: (Praise be to Allah who has taken away the harm from me) by the exit of waste that would cause illness if retained, and (given me health) by preserving the property of food which if all retained or exited would be a cause of destruction. And the Messenger of Allah (PBUH) said upon leaving: 'Your forgiveness' which is a metaphor for acknowledging shortcomings in achieving the right to thank for the blessing of feeding and the management of the property of food and facilitation of the exit of harm.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage enumerates various disliked and prohibited actions while on the toilet. One should not engage in any form of dhikr, respond to sneezes, greetings, or adhan, nor look at private parts or excrement, nor spit, blow nose, clear throat, look around, fidget, look up, or sit too long. The reason for avoiding prolonged sitting is that it causes hemorrhoids and liver pain. One should exit with the right foot, as the right is preferred for good actions. After leaving, one should say the prescribed supplication: 'Alhamdulillah...' and also 'Ghufranak' as taught by the Prophet. The commentary explains these supplications acknowledge Allah's blessings in removing harmful waste and preserving beneficial food. These etiquettes are part of the comprehensive adab of the toilet in Islam.

Key Terms
غفرانك (ghufranak): Your forgiveness; a supplication acknowledging sin.
الحمد الله الذي أذهب عني الأذى وعافاني (alhamdulillah alladhi adhhaba anni al-adha wa afani): Praise be to Allah who has taken away the harm from me and given me health.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which foot should you use to exit the toilet?
A) Left foot
B) Right foot
C) Either foot
D) Both feet together

Key Takeaway
After using the toilet, one should step out with the right foot and recite the supplication taught by the Prophet (PBUH).

Exit Supplication from Toilet: Gratitude and Forgiveness
Explanation of the supplication upon leaving toilet

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended supplication after leaving the toilet and explain the meaning of 'Ghufranak' in this context.

Core Idea
Praise Allah for removing harm and granting health, then seek His forgiveness for shortcomings in gratitude.

After answering the call of nature, a Muslim is encouraged to express gratitude to Allah for the ability to relieve oneself and for preserving health. This passage explains the dua to be recited upon leaving the toilet, combining praise and seeking forgiveness. Understanding the wisdom behind these words deepens our appreciation of Allah's blessings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended dua
Find the Arabic text of the dua: 'Alhamdulillah alladhi adhhaba 'anni al-adha wa 'aafani' and 'Ghufranak'. State that both are sunnah after leaving the toilet.
The dua begins with praise, not a direct request.
2
Analyze the first part: praise and gratitude
Understand that 'al-adha' (harm) refers to waste that could cause illness if retained, and 'aafani' (gave me health) refers to preserving the beneficial property of food. This shows gratitude for both excretion and retention in balance.
The commentary highlights the medical wisdom: complete retention or complete excretion would cause harm.
3
Analyze the second part: 'Ghufranak'
Recognize that 'Ghufranak' is a supplication for forgiveness, but in this context it acknowledges that one cannot fully thank Allah for the blessings of food, digestion, and elimination.
It is a metaphor (kinayah) for admitting inadequacy in gratitude.
4
Apply the ruling
State that reciting this dua is sunnah (recommended) after leaving the toilet. It is not obligatory, but meritorious.
One may recite either the full phrase or just 'Ghufranak' as reported from the Prophet.
5
Reflect on the spirit of the supplication
Consider how this dua combines outward gratitude with inward humility, recognizing that even our ability to thank is a gift from Allah.
This teaches that all blessings, including basic bodily functions, require acknowledgment of Allah's favor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ثم يقول) بعد الخروج (الحمد الله الذي أذهب عني الأذى) بخروج الفضلات الممرضة بحبسها وعافاني) بإبقاء خاصية الغذاء الذي لو أمسك كله أو خرج لكان مظنة الهلاك، وقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عند خروجه غفرانك وهو كناية عن الإعتراف بالقصور عن بلوغ حق شكر نعمة الإطعام وتصريف خاصية الغذاء وتسهيل خروج الأذى
His saying: (Then he says) after leaving (Praise be to Allah who has taken away the harm from me) by the exit of waste that would cause illness if retained, and (given me health) by preserving the property of food which if all retained or exited would be a cause of destruction. And the Messenger of Allah (PBUH) said upon leaving: 'Your forgiveness' which is a metaphor for acknowledging shortcomings in achieving the right to thank for the blessing of feeding and the management of the property of food and facilitation of the exit of harm.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage focuses on the dua to be recited after leaving the toilet. The first part is 'Alhamdulillah alladhi adhhaba 'anni al-adha wa 'aafani', praising Allah for removing harmful waste and granting health. The commentary explains the wisdom: retention of waste causes illness, and complete excretion would also be harmful, so the balance is a blessing. The second part is the Prophet's dua 'Ghufranak', meaning 'I seek Your forgiveness', which acknowledges that one cannot fully thank Allah for these blessings. This dua combines gratitude and repentance. It is recommended to say these words after leaving the toilet.

Key Terms
الأذى (al-adha): Harm; here refers to the waste that causes illness if retained.
خاصية الغذاء (khassiyat al-ghidha): The property of food; its nutritional benefit that is extracted and retained.
غفرانك (ghufranak): Your forgiveness; an invocation for pardon.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'Ghufranak' in the context of leaving the toilet?
A) A request for protection from impurity.
B) An acknowledgment of inability to thank Allah properly for His blessings.
C) A request for more food.
D) A prayer for health.

Key Takeaway
Recite the dua after leaving the toilet to praise Allah for health and seek forgiveness for any inadequacy in gratitude.

Uncovering the 'Awrah in Solitude: The Hanafi Dispute
Critical refinement on the ruling of covering 'awrah in solitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on uncovering the 'awrah in solitude and identify which is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Uncovering the 'awrah in solitude is at minimum makruh tanzihi, with some scholars holding it makruh tahrimi.

This lesson examines the ruling on uncovering one's private parts while using the toilet, even when alone. The Hashiya clarifies a critical refinement: there are two positions within the Hanafi school on this matter. Understanding this ikhtilaf is essential for proper practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act being ruled
Pinpoint the act: uncovering the 'awrah (private parts) while in solitude (i.e., in the toilet) without a pressing need.
The 'awrah includes both front and back.
2
Analyze the hadith evidence
Examine the hadith of Anas ibn Malik (RA): the Prophet (peace be upon him) would not lift his garment until close to the ground when relieving himself. This indicates that there is a prohibition before necessity arises.
Hadith narrated by al-Tirmidhi, classified as hasan.
3
Recognize the two Hanafi positions
According to the text, there are two views: 1) it is makruh tahrimi (prohibited) to uncover the 'awrah in solitude, and 2) it is only makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked). The ikhtilaf arises from differing interpretations of 'necessity' and the strength of the hadith.
The author mentions that the matn's statement 'not permissible' refers to before nearing the ground, and after that it falls under the ikhtilaf.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The more lenient position (makruh tanzihi) is likely the relied upon opinion for fatwa, as indicated by the phrase 'على الخلاف' and the context of the commentary.
This is from later Hanafi fatwa works.
5
Apply to practice
In practice, one should cover the 'awrah as soon as possible, even when alone, and avoid unnecessary exposure. If there is a genuine need (e.g., medical), it becomes permissible.
The hadith of Anas shows the sunnah of delaying uncovering until just before sitting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجيب عورته قائماً وذكر الله فلا يحمد إذا عطس، ولا يشمّت عاطساً، ولا يرد سلاماً ولا مؤذناً ولا ينظر لعورته ولا إلى الخارج منها ولا يبصق ولا يتمخط ولا يتنحنح، ولا يكثر الإلتفات، ولا يعبث ببدنه ولا يرفع بصره إلى السماء ولا يطيل الجلوس لأنه يورث الباسور، ووجع الكبد ويخرج من الخلاء برجله اليمنى لأنها أحق بالتقدم لنعمة الإنصراف الأذى ومحل الشياطين (ثم يقول) بعد الخروج (الحمد الله الذي أذهب عني الأذى) بخروج الفضلات الممرضة بحبسها وعافاني) بإبقاء خاصية الغذاء الذي لو أمسك كله أو خرج لكان مظنة الهلاك، وقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عند خروجه غفرانك وهو كناية عن الإعتراف بالقصور عن بلوغ حق شكر نعمة الإطعام وتصريف خاصية الغذاء وتسهيل خروج الأذى عن تحرزاً في [؟] عن كشف العورة بغير ضرورة لقول أنس رضي الله تعالى عنه : كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا أراد الحاجة لم يرفع ثوبه حتى يدنو من الأرض رواه الترمذي بسند حسن قال الأبياري في شرح الجامع الصغير : محله ما لم يخف التنجس والأرفع بقدر الحاجة اهـ وقال الطيبي : يستوي فيه الصحراء والبنيان لأن كشف العورة لا يجوز إلا عند الحاجة يعني الضرورة، ولا ضرورة قبل القرب من الأرض، وعدم الجواز أحد قولين في الخلوة عندنا وشمل كلام المصنف كشفها بعد الفراغ فيكره إما تحريماً أو تنزيهاً على الخلاف في كشف العورة في الخلوة، ويستحب غسل يده بعد الفراغ وإن ظهرت بطهارة المحل مبالغة في التنظيف. قوله : (وذكر الله الخ) بل يكره مطلق الكلام حال قضاء الحاجة والمجامعة إلا لحاجة تفوت بالتأخير كتحذير نحو أعمى من سقوط. قوله : فلا يحمد إذا عطس الخ)، وله أن يفعل ذلك نفسه من غير تلفظ بلسانه قوله : ولا ينظر لعورته فإنه خلاف الأدب، وكذا الأولى عدم نظر أحد الزوجين إلى عورة الآخر وكما يندب له الستر يندب تغطية رأسه، وخفض صوته قال علي رضي الله عنه : من أكثر النظر إلى سوأته عوقب بالنسيان اهـ، وقيل: من أكثر مسها ابتلي بالزنا. قوله : (ولا) إلى الخارج فإنه يورث النسيان، وهو مستقذر شرعاً ولا داعيةً له. قوله: (ولا) يبصق لأنه يصفر الأسنان قوله : ( ولا يتمخط) لامتلاء أنفه بالرائحة الكريهة. قوله: (ولا يكثر الإلتفات الخ لأنه محل حضور الشياطين، فلا يفعل فيه ما لا حاجة إليه. قوله: (ولا يرفع بصره إلى السماء لأنه محل التفكر في آياتها، وليس هذا محله. قوله: (لأنه يورث الباسور ووجع الكبد) روي ذلك عن لقمان الحكيم، ولأنه محل الشياطين، فيستحب الإسراع بالخروج منه قوله عن الأذى أي عن محل إخراجه. قوله : (بخروج الفضلات متعلق بأذهب وقوله بحبسها متعلق بالممرضة. قوله: (غفرانك) منصوب بمحذوف أي أطلب منك غفرانك لي أي ستر ذنبي أو محوه وهو من باب حسنات الأبرار سيئات المقربين قوله : (وهو كناية عن الإعتراف فكأنه يقول يا رب اغفر لي ما قصرت فيه من الوفاء بشكر هذه النعمة قوله : (نعمة الإطعام إضافته للبيان . لسلامة البدن من الآلام أو عن عدم الذكر باللسان حال التخلي .
He covers his private parts standing, and remembers Allah, so he does not praise if he sneezes, nor does he respond to a sneezer, nor return a greeting, nor call the adhan, nor look at his private parts nor at what comes out of them, nor spit, nor blow his nose, nor clear his throat, nor turn around excessively, nor fidget with his body, nor raise his gaze to the sky, nor prolong sitting because it causes hemorrhoids and liver pain. He exits the toilet with his right foot because it is more deserving to go forward due to the blessing of departing from harm and the abode of devils. Then after exiting he says: 'Praise be to Allah who has removed from me harm' due to the exit of waste that would be harmful if retained, 'and given me well-being' by preserving the nutritional benefit of food which if entirely retained or excreted would be a cause of destruction. The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said upon exiting: 'Your forgiveness' which is a metaphor for admitting shortcomings in fulfilling the right of thanking for the blessing of feeding, etc. Out of caution against uncovering the private parts without necessity due to the hadith of Anas...
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi is a critical refinement on the rulings of entering and exiting the toilet from Maraqi al-Falah. It lists numerous recommended and prohibited actions: covering the 'awrah, remembering Allah, avoiding speech except for necessity, not looking at private parts or excrement, no spitting or nose-blowing, no excessive looking around, no gazing at the sky, and not prolonging sitting due to health harms. It also states the sunnah of entering with the left foot and exiting with the right, and the supplications. The commentary discusses the ruling on uncovering the 'awrah in solitude: there are two opinions in the Hanafi school, makruh tahrimi and makruh tanzihi, and the author notes that the matn's ruling of 'not permissible' before nearing the ground is based on the hadith of Anas. The commentary also explains the supplication 'Ghufranak' as an admission of inability to adequately thank Allah.

Key Terms
عورة ('awrah): The private parts that must be covered; in this context, the parts that should not be unnecessarily exposed even in solitude.
خلوة (khalwah): Privacy, being alone. Here, it refers to the state of being in a private place such as a toilet.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this lesson, what is the relied-upon ruling on uncovering the 'awrah in solitude?
A) It is haram in all cases.
B) It is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked).
C) It is makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked).
D) It is permissible (mubah).

Key Takeaway
Uncovering the 'awrah in solitude is disliked (makruh), with the preferred position being makruh tanzihi, but necessity can make it permissible.

Speech While Relieving Oneself: Disliked Except in Need
Listing prohibited speech acts during relieving oneself and exception for necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the general prohibition of speech during relieving oneself and identify the exceptions.

Core Idea
All speech while using the toilet is disliked, including dhikr and responding to others, unless a need arises that cannot be delayed.

This lesson covers the etiquettes of refraining from speech while relieving oneself. The Hashiya lists several actions that are discouraged: praising a sneeze, responding to a sneezer, returning greetings, and calling the adhan. The key principle is that general speech is makruh, but if a genuine need like warning a blind person from falling exists, it becomes permissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling on speech
The text states that speech (dhikr, responding, etc.) is disliked (makruh) during relieving oneself and during intercourse.
This includes verbal dhikr, though silent remembrance is fine.
2
Recognize the list of prohibited speech acts
The matn mentions: not praising (tahmeed) after a sneeze, not responding to a sneezer, not returning a greeting, not calling the adhan. These are specific examples.
The phrase 'وذكر الله فلا يحمد' indicates that even though dhikr is good, one should not verbalize it in this situation.
3
Understand the exception for necessity
The text says: 'except for a need that would be missed if delayed, like warning a blind person of a fall.' This is the only exception.
The need must be genuine and time-sensitive.
4
Apply the ruling to silent dhikr
The commentary notes that one may do dhikr in the heart without moving the tongue, avoiding the disliked speech.
This is a concession for those habituated to dhikr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بمحذوف أي أطلب منك غفرانك لي أي ستر ذنبي أو محوه وهو من باب حسنات الأبرار سيئات المقربين قوله : (وهو كناية عن الإعتراف فكأنه يقول يا رب اغفر لي ما قصرت فيه من الوفاء بشكر هذه النعمة قوله : (نعمة الإطعام إضافته للبيان . لسلامة البدن من الآلام أو عن عدم الذكر باللسان حال التخلي .
He covers his private parts standing, and remembers Allah, so he does not praise if he sneezes, nor does he respond to a sneezer, nor return a greeting, nor call the adhan, nor look at his private parts nor at what comes out of them, nor spit, nor blow his nose, nor clear his throat, nor turn around excessively, nor fidget with his body, nor raise his gaze to the sky, nor prolong sitting because it causes hemorrhoids and liver pain. He exits the toilet with his right foot because it is more deserving to go forward due to the blessing of departing from harm and the abode of devils. Then after exiting he says: 'Praise be to Allah who has removed from me harm' due to the exit of waste that would be harmful if retained, 'and given me well-being' by preserving the nutritional benefit of food which if entirely retained or excreted would be a cause of destruction. The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said upon exiting: 'Your forgiveness' which is a metaphor for admitting shortcomings in fulfilling the right of thanking for the blessing of feeding, etc. Out of caution against uncovering the private parts without necessity due to the hadith of Anas...
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi is a critical refinement on the rulings of entering and exiting the toilet from Maraqi al-Falah. It lists numerous recommended and prohibited actions: covering the 'awrah, remembering Allah, avoiding speech except for necessity, not looking at private parts or excrement, no spitting or nose-blowing, no excessive looking around, no gazing at the sky, and not prolonging sitting due to health harms. It also states the sunnah of entering with the left foot and exiting with the right, and the supplications. The commentary discusses the ruling on uncovering the 'awrah in solitude: there are two opinions in the Hanafi school, makruh tahrimi and makruh tanzihi, and the author notes that the matn's ruling of 'not permissible' before nearing the ground is based on the hadith of Anas. The commentary also explains the supplication 'Ghufranak' as an admission of inability to adequately thank Allah.

Key Terms
يُشمّت (yushammit): To respond to a sneezer by saying يَرْحَمُكَ اللَّهُ (may Allah have mercy on you).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is it permissible to speak while using the toilet according to this passage?
A) When saying dhikr or praising Allah.
B) When there is an urgent need that cannot be delayed.
C) When greeting someone who enters.
D) It is never permissible.

Key Takeaway
All speech during relieving oneself is disliked except for urgent needs that cannot be postponed.

Physical Conduct in the Toilet: Prohibited and Disliked Actions
List of physical actions to avoid during relieving oneself with rationales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the physical actions that are disliked or prohibited while using the toilet and explain their rationale.

Core Idea
Avoid looking at private parts, spitting, blowing nose, excessive turning, fidgeting, raising gaze to sky, and prolonging sitting due to health and spiritual harms.

This lesson details the recommended and prohibited physical actions when relieving oneself. The Hashiya lists several behaviors to avoid: looking at one's private parts or excrement, spitting, blowing nose, clearing throat, excessive turning, fidgeting, raising the gaze to the sky, and prolonging sitting. Each has a specific rationale, from health consequences like hemorrhoids to spiritual reasons like the presence of devils.

Guided Path
1
Identify the list of prohibited physical actions
The text enumerates: not looking at one's 'awrah, not looking at what comes out, not spitting, not blowing nose, not clearing throat, not turning around excessively, not fidgeting, not raising gaze to sky, not prolonging sitting.
These are all makruh (disliked) or haram depending on the act.
2
Understand the rationale for each
Some are for health: spitting makes teeth yellow, blowing nose in a smelly place, prolonging sitting causes hemorrhoids and liver pain. Others are spiritual: looking at private parts leads to forgetfulness, raising gaze is inappropriate for place of devils.
The text quotes a saying of Ali: 'Whoever looks excessively at his private parts is punished with forgetfulness.'
3
Note the hadith evidence for not raising gaze
The text says it is not the place for contemplating the heavens’ signs. This is an adab.
This is based on the principle of avoiding excessive curiosity in a filthy place.
4
Apply the ruling on looking at spouse's 'awrah
The commentary adds that it is also better for spouses not to look at each other's 'awrah, though it is not haram.
This is an extension of the general etiquette of covering.

Etiquettes of Entering and Exiting the Toilet
Rulings on foot placement and supplications for entering and exiting the toilet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recite and explain the rulings on entering and exiting the toilet, including foot placement and supplications.

Core Idea
Enter with the left foot, exit with the right, and recite the prescribed supplications for entering and exiting the toilet.

This lesson covers the sunnah actions when entering and exiting the toilet: entering with the left foot and exiting with the right, along with the appropriate supplications. The wisdom behind exiting with the right foot is that it is more deserving to go forward for the blessing of being relieved from harm and leaving the abode of devils. The exit supplication 'Ghufranak' is explained as an admission of human shortcoming in thanking Allah for the ability to relieve oneself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sunnah of entering and exiting
The text states: enter with the left foot, exit with the right foot. This is based on the general principle that the right side is preferable for good things.
The left is used for entering because it is a place of impurity.
2
Learn the entrance and exit supplications
The text gives the exit supplication: 'الحمد لله الذي أذهب عني الأذى وعافاني'. The Prophet also said 'غفرانك' upon exiting.
The entrance supplication is not quoted here but is known from other hadith.
3
Understand the meaning of 'غفرانك'
The commentary explains it as an acknowledgment of inability to thank Allah properly for the blessing of relieving oneself. It is like saying 'I seek Your forgiveness.'
This is from the category of 'حسنات الأبرار سيئات المقربين' (the good deeds of the righteous are sins of the near ones).
4
Note the wisdom behind the rulings
The text explains: the right foot is given precedence because it is a blessing to leave the place of harm and devils.
This connects etiquettes to spiritual meanings.

Definition and Pillars of Wudu
Defines wudu and introduces pillars

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and legal definition of wudu and identify its four pillars according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Wudu is a specific legal purification with four obligatory pillars; its denial as fard qat'i constitutes disbelief.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi lays the foundation for understanding wudu by clarifying its meaning in language and law, then immediately introduces its four pillars (arkan). The author also clarifies a common misconception and distinguishes the grades of obligation (fard qat'i vs zanni). Understanding these basics is essential before diving into the detailed rulings of wudu.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish linguistic vs legal definition
First, extract the linguistic meaning (beauty/cleanliness) and then the legal meaning (specific washing/mashing). Note how the legal definition adds the purpose of 'tahjil' for divine service.
The word wudu' can also refer to the water used when pronounced with fatha only.
2
Identify the four pillars
From the text, note that the pillars are four, beginning with washing the face. The passage only mentions the first pillar explicitly; subsequent study will cover the others.
Pillars (arkan) are obligatory acts whose omission invalidates wudu.
3
Understand fard qat'i vs zanni
The text divides fard into two types: qat'i (definitive proof, denial=kufr) and zanni (definitive proof but ambiguous, called 'practical'). Recognize the Hanafi distinction.
This classification affects the gravity of denial, not the obligation itself.
4
Recognize the unique ghurrah/tahjil
Note that wudu itself is not exclusive to this ummah; previous prophets also performed it. The unique feature is the radiant marks on the forehead and limbs on Judgement Day.
This refutes the common misunderstanding that wudu is a unique act of worship.

Section on the rulings of wudu. Wudu: with damm on the waw and fatha, it is a verbal noun, and with fatha only it means the water used for wudu. Linguistically it is derived from wada'a (beauty, brightness, cleanliness). One says: 'wadu'a ar-rajul' meaning he became handsome. Legally it is a specific cleanliness because in the linguistic sense it includes the tahjil (brightness of limbs) for serving the Master. And it is placed before ghusl because Allah gave it precedence. It has a cause, condition, ruling, pillar, and description. The pillars of wudu are four, which are its obligatory acts: first is washing the face. His statement: the organs of wudu are cleaned in this world and beautified in the Hereafter... The correct view is that wudu is not a distinctive characteristic of this ummah; rather what is distinctive is the ghurrah and tahjil (brightness of forehead and limbs). Al-Allamah Nuh mentioned that, and in Sharh al-Mishkat it is said that the ghurrah and tahjil are specific to prophets and this ummah among all nations. Wudu was made obligatory in Makkah and its verse was revealed in Madinah as continuous divine confirmation... The obligatory (fard) is of two types: qat'i (definitive) established by a definitive proof, denial of which is disbelief; and zanni (speculative) established by a definitive proof but with some ambiguity, called practical.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi defines wudu and explains its linguistic and legal meanings. The author notes that wudu is not a unique feature of this ummah (unlike the ghurrah and tahjil on Judgement Day). The pillars (arkan) of wudu are four, and the text begins discussing the first: washing the face. The commentary also clarifies the classification of fard into qat'i (clear, definitive) and zanni (speculative, but still binding). The phrase 'ghurrah wa tahjil' refers to the brightness of the forehead and limbs from the water of wudu on the Day of Resurrection.

Key Terms
الوضوء (al-wudu'): Legal purification by washing specific limbs; linguistically from wada'a (beauty, cleanliness).
الغرة والتحجيل (al-ghurrah wa al-tahjil): Brightness on the face and hands/feet from wudu, visible on Judgement Day.
فرض قطعي (fard qat'i): Obligation established by definitive evidence; denial is kufr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of denying a fard qat'i?
A) It is sinful but not disbelief
B) It is disbelief (kufr)
C) It is permissible if done ignorantly
D) It requires expiation only

Key Takeaway
Wudu is a fundamental act with four pillars; its obligation is definitively established, and its unique feature for this ummah is the radiant glow on Judgement Day, not the act itself.

Washing the Face in Wudu: Definition, Proof, and Minimum Requirement
Explains the first pillar of wudu with proof and definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ghusl for the face, identify the Quranic proof, and apply the minimum requirement of two drops for valid washing.

Core Idea
Washing the face in wudu requires flowing water that drips; mere wetting is insufficient.

In this lesson, we examine the first pillar of wudu: washing the face. Al-Tahtawi's hashia provides a detailed definition of ghusl, clarifies the boundaries of the face, and addresses an internal Hanafi difference on whether mere pouring suffices. Understanding this ruling is essential for valid wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is washing the face, which is one of the four fara'id (obligatory acts) of wudu. Its hukm level is fard al-ayn, established by a decisive proof.
Denial of this fard entails disbelief (kufr).
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the Quranic verse in Surah al-Ma'idah 5:6: 'Wash your faces.' The imperative form indicates obligation according to Hanafi usul.
This verse is qat'i al-thubut wa al-dalalah.
3
Define ghusl legally
The passage defines ghusl as 'flowing of water over the part such that it drips.' Mere pouring without dripping is insufficient according to the majority.
Abu Yusuf differs: he accepts mere pouring.
4
Specify the minimum
The minimum valid washing is two drops that drip from the face. This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view, though al-Fath mentions one drop as sufficient.
The 'most correct' (al-ashah) position is two drops.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Abu Yusuf holds that mere pouring without dripping suffices, but the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) require dripping. The mufta bihi is the majority view.
This difference does not affect the overall obligation, only the minimum requirement.

His statement: (and they are its obligatory acts) The fard is of two types: categorical, which is established by a decisive proof leading to intuitive knowledge, and its denier is a disbeliever; and speculative, which is established by a decisive proof but contains some ambiguity, and it is called practical. The three [pillars] and wiping. Allah Most High said: 'Wash your faces.' Al-ghusl (washing) with fath of the ghayn is a verbal noun, with damm it is a noun, and with kasr it is what is used for washing like soap and its like. Al-ghusl is the flowing of water over the part such that it drips. Its minimum is two drops according to the most correct view, and mere flowing without dripping is insufficient. The face is what a person faces with. (Its limit) i.e., the entirety of the face in length from the beginning of the surface of the forehead, whether there is hair on it or not. The forehead is what is framed by the two eyebrows. (to) And it is that whose omission invalidates permissibility, and its ruling is like the first except that its denier is not declared a disbeliever. If one considers the origin of washing and wiping, it is from the first type; if one considers the estimation, it is from the second. Know that proofs are four types: first, decisive in transmission and meaning, like Quranic verses and explicit mutawatir hadith that admit no interpretation from any aspect; second, decisive in transmission but speculative in meaning, like verses and hadith that are subject to interpretation; third, speculative in transmission but decisive in meaning, like explicit khabar wahid; fourth, speculative in transmission and meaning, like khabar wahid that carry multiple meanings. The first yields certainty, the second yields probability, the third yields obligation and prohibition disliked with karahah tanzihiyyah, and the fourth yields sunnah and recommendation. Sometimes fard is used to include categorical and practical, and wajib is used to mean practical fard as well. Hence some verifiers said: It is the stronger of the two types of wajib and the weaker of the two types of fard. Then fard itself is of two types: fard al-ayn and fard al-kifayah. The first is obligatory on every individual and is not dropped by the act of some, like wudu for example. The second is obligatory on the collective of those obligated, not on each individual specifically, so it is dropped from all by the act of some, like listening to the Quran, memorizing it, returning greetings, responding to sneezes, washing the deceased, praying over him, enjoining good and forbidding evil, and jihad unless there is a general call to arms, in which case it becomes fard al-ayn. Then all fard kifayah: their reward is for the one who directly performs it, and the sin of abandoning it is upon all. The necessary consequence of abandoning a fard is that the act is valid in any case, but sin if intentional; the necessary consequence of abandoning a wajib is karahah tahrimiyyah (prohibitive dislike) if intentional, otherwise sahw prostration if in prayer; the necessary consequence of abandoning a sunnah or mustahabb is karahah tanzihiyyah (disliked but not sinful) if intentional, otherwise nothing. His statement: (and with damm it is a noun) i.e., the noun of the verbal noun. The difference between the verbal noun and its noun is that the verbal noun indicates the event directly, while its noun indicates it indirectly. It is also applied to washing the entire body, and it is a name for the water used for ghusl. His statement: (flowing of water over the part) whereas wiping is touching, as in al-Hidayah. His statement: (such that it drips) meaning that it actually drips, or would drip if not dried. This is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). According to Abu Yusuf, mere pouring over the limb suffices even if it does not drip. His statement: (in the most correct) and apparently in al-Fath one drop suffices. His statement: (what) a person faces (with) i.e., what falls under the gaze when facing, which is the opposite of the two faces. His statement: (its limit) i.e., the face linguistically and legally, as in al-Qahistani. The limit of a thing is its end, as in al-Sihah. His statement: (from the beginning of the surface of the forehead) i.e., from the top of the forehead. His statement: (whether there is hair on it or not) he indicates by this that for a bald, hairless, or partially bald person, washing the face includes what is mentioned. His statement: (and the forehead) in al-Qamus is what touches the ground during prostration and the area between
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya, focusing on the first pillar of wudu: washing the face. The matn states that the pillars (arkan) of wudu are four, which are also called fara'id (obligatory acts). The hashia then identifies two types of fard: qat'i (categorical, established by decisive proof, denial entails disbelief) and zanni (speculative, established by decisive proof but with ambiguity, also called 'amali/practical). The author then presents the Quranic proof from Surah al-Ma'ida (5:6): 'Wash your faces.' He defines ghusl (washing) linguistically and legally: it is the flowing of water over the part such that it drips. The minimum for valid washing is two drops according to the relied-upon view, though some say one drop suffices. The face is defined as the area from the top of the forehead to the chin, and between the two ears (in a later section not fully shown). The forehead includes the area framed by the two eyebrows. The presence or absence of hair does not affect the obligation; all must wash the entire face area. The author then undertakes a detailed discussion of the categories of legal obligation: fard (both 'ayn and kifayah), wajib, sunnah, and mustahabb, and the consequences of omitting each. He clarifies that fard and wajib are distinct in the Hanafi school: fard requires a decisive proof (qat'i al-thubut wa al-dalalah), while wajib may be based on zanni proof. Omitting a fard intentionally incurs sin but the act may still be valid (e.g., if done forgetfully), while omitting a wajib intentionally brings karahah tahrimiyyah (prohibitive dislike) and requires sajdah al-sahw in prayer. The commentary also refines the definitions, such as the difference between ghusl and mas'h (wiping). The author concludes with the specific definition of the forehead from al-Qamus.

Key Terms
الغسل (al-ghusl): Legally: flowing water over the part such that it drips (according to the majority); minimum two drops
الوجه (al-wajh): The face, from the top of the forehead to the chin, and between the two ears; the forehead is what is framed by the two eyebrows
الفرض (al-farḍ): A legal obligation established by a decisive proof (qat'i al-thubut wa al-dalalah), denial of which renders one a disbeliever
الواجب (al-wājib): A legal obligation established by a speculative proof (zanni), denial of which does not entail disbelief but omission is sinful

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Simply pouring water over the face regardless of dripping
B) Flowing water that drips, with a minimum of two drops
C) Only one drop of water is sufficient
D) The face must be rubbed with water, dripping is not required

Key Takeaway
Washing the face in wudu requires water to flow and drip, with a minimum of two drops, based on the Quranic command; mere pouring without dripping is insufficient.

Defining Ghusl and the Boundaries of the Face in Wudu
Commentary defining ghusl and face limits for wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of ghusl, identify the precise boundaries of the face in wudu, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the temporal white area.

Core Idea
Washing the face in wudu requires water to drip (minimum two drops) and includes the entire area from forehead to chin and between earlobes, including the temporal white area.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi builds on the Qur'anic command to wash the face in wudu. It clarifies what constitutes valid washing (ghusl) and precisely defines the face's limits. In doing so, it also addresses a subtle intra-Hanafi difference on whether a beard-covered area near the ear must be washed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The act is washing the face in wudu. Its ruling is fard (obligatory) based on the clear Qur'anic command 'fa-ghsilu wujūhakum'. Understand that this obligation is established by a qat'i dalil (Qur'an).
The obligation is fard 'ayn on every Muslim when performing wudu.
2
Understand the legal definition of ghusl
Examine the Arabic term 'al-ghusl' with its three vowel forms. Legally, ghusl requires water to flow over the body part and drip; mere pouring without dripping is insufficient. The minimum is two drops according to the most correct view (al-ashahh).
The intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf holds that mere pouring suffices, but the relied-upon view requires dripping.
3
Delineate the face boundaries in length
The face in length extends from the top of the forehead (the surface of the jabha, which is the area between the temples) down to the bottom of the chin (the meeting point of the two jawbones). The forehead is included whether or not hair grows on it. The beard area is part of the face; for those with thick beards, washing the surface is sufficient; for thin beards, water must reach the skin.
The jaw (lahy) is the growth area of the beard, above the teeth bone for a beardless person.
4
Delineate the face boundaries in width
The face in width is between the two earlobes. A small part of the earlobes themselves is included because they are connected to the face.
The ears are not part of the face but a part is included out of necessity.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on the temporal white area
The white area between the cheek strap (al-adhar) and the ear is part of the face. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, it is obligatory to wash in all cases. Abu Yusuf held that if the beard covers this area, the obligation is dropped. The relied-upon view (al-sahih) is that it remains obligatory, as it is within the defined boundaries of the face.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf): following Abu Yusuf may leave a part of the face unwashable, potentially invalidating wudu according to the majority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة قال تعالى: فاغسلوا وجوهكم والغسل بفتح الغين مصدر غسلته وبالضم الإسم، وبالكسر ما يغسل به من صابون ونحوه والغسل إسالة الماء على المحل بحيث يتقاطر، وأقله قطرتان في الأصح ولا تكفي الإسالة بدون التقاطر. والوجه ما يواجه به الإنسان (وحده) أي جملة الوجه طولاً من مبدأ سطح الجبهة سواء كان به شعر أم لا والجبهة ما اكتنفه الجبينان إلى أسفل الذقن وهي مجمع لحييه واللحى منبت اللحية فوق عظم الأسنان لمن ليست له لحية كثيفة وفي حقه إلى ما لا في البشرة من الوجه (وحده) أي الوجه عرضاً ما بين شحمة الأذنين ويدخل في الغايتين جزء منهما لاتصاله بالفرض والبياض الذي بين العذار والأذن فيفترض غسله في الصحيح وعن أبي يوسف سقوطه بنبات اللحية.
The Book of Purity. Allah Almighty said: 'Wash your faces.' Ghusl (with fatha on ghayn) is the verbal noun of 'I washed it'; with damm it is the noun; with kasr it is the tool used for washing, like soap and the like. Ghusl is the flowing of water over the place such that it drips. Its minimum is two drops according to the most correct view, and mere pouring without dripping is not sufficient. The face is what one faces others with. Its limit (i.e., the entirety of the face) in length: from the beginning of the surface of the forehead, whether there is hair on it or not. The forehead is what is flanked by the two temples, down to the lower part of the chin, which is the meeting point of the two jawbones. The jaw (lahy) is the growth area of the beard above the teeth bone for one who does not have a thick beard. And in his case, it extends to what touches the skin of the face. Its limit (i.e., the face) in width: between the two ear lobes. A part of the two ends is included due to its connection to the obligatory area. The white area between the cheek strap and the ear is obligatory to wash according to the correct view, and from Abu Yusuf it is dropped due to the growth of the beard.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the Qur'anic verse for wudu, defining key terms and legal boundaries. The author first explains the word 'ghusl' (washing): it has three vowelized forms—with fatha it is a verbal noun (the act of washing), with damm it is a noun (the act of washing or the entire body wash), and with kasr it is the instrument (e.g., soap). The legal definition of ghusl is the flowing of water over the body part such that it drips; mere pouring without drips does not count. The minimum amount is two drops, according to the preferred view (al-ashahh). Then the face (wajh) is defined. In length, it extends from the top of the forehead (where hair normally begins) to the bottom of the chin (the meeting of the jawbones). The forehead is the area between the two temples. The chin is the lower jaw area. The jaw (lahy) is the growth area of the beard, which for men without beards is above the teeth. For those with thick beards, the obligation is limited to the surface; for those with thin beards, water must reach the skin. In width, the face is from one earlobe to the other, including a small part of the ears themselves because they are connected. The white area between the cheek strap (adhādhār) and the ear is obligatory to wash (this is the temporal area). There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that if a beard grows over this area, the obligation is dropped, but the correct view (al-sahih) is that it remains obligatory.

Key Terms
الغسل (al-ghusl): The flowing of water over a body part such that it drips; minimum two drops. Also the verbal noun of washing.
الوجه (al-wajh): The face: from the top of the forehead to the bottom of the chin in length, and from one earlobe to the other in width.
الجبهة (al-jabha): The forehead, flanked by the two temples, from the hairline to the eyebrows.
البياض (al-bayād): The white area between the cheek strap (al-adhar) and the ear; part of the face according to the relied-upon view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most correct view (al-ashahh), what is the minimum number of drops required for ghusl in wudu?
A) A single drop is sufficient if water flows
B) Two drops are required
C) No minimum; any amount of water suffices
D) A handful must be used

Key Takeaway
Washing the face in wudu requires valid ghusl (dripping water, minimum two drops) and encompasses the entire area from forehead to chin and between earlobes, including the temporal white area, according to the majority Hanafi view.

Definition and Boundaries of the Face in Wudu
Definition of the face boundaries in wudu with a dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of the face in wudu, identify its boundaries, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the white skin between the sideburn and ear.

Core Idea
The face in wudu extends from the forehead hairline to the chin, and widthwise between the earlobes; the white skin between sideburn and ear is wajib to wash.

Washing the face is a fard act of wudu, but where exactly does the face begin and end? This lesson unpacks the precise boundaries defined by the Hanafi school, including a key internal disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf about an area often overlooked. Understanding these limits ensures your wudu is complete according to the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its definition
Locate the definition of the face (al-wajh) in the source. Note its length (from forehead hairline to chin) and width (between earlobes).
The chin (al-dhaqn) is the meeting point of the jawbones.
2
Analyze the components of the definition
Break down the definition into its constituent parts: the area including the beard growth for those with thick beard, and the requirement for the skin to be visible.
For those with a thick beard, only the visible skin must be washed; the internal hair growth does not need to be reached.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
Note the disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa regarding the white skin between the sideburn (al-'idhar) and the ear. Abu Yusuf held it drops if the beard is thick; Abu Hanifa held it remains wajib.
The passage explicitly states 'فيفترض غسله في الصحيح' (its washing is obligatory in the correct view).
4
Apply the tarjih (preferred view)
Understand that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's, making that area wajib to wash regardless of beard thickness.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

The commentary also discusses the hair removal area (tahdhif) between the ear and forehead, stating it is part of the head, not face. The passage emphasizes that the obligation covers the entire face within these boundaries.

الذقن (al-dhaqn): The chin, the lowest part of the face at the meeting of the jawbones.
اللحية (al-lihya): Beard hair on the cheeks and chin.
العذار (al-'idhar): The sideburn area between the ear and the forehead.

Obligation of Washing Both Elbows in Wudu: Text and Implication
Proving the obligation of washing both elbows

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the evidence from the Quran and consensus for washing both elbows in wudu, and apply the rule for washing extra limbs attached to the hand.

Core Idea
Both elbows are obligatory: one by explicit text, the other by implication and consensus.

In this passage, the commentator explains how the obligation to wash the hands in wudu includes both elbows, using the Quranic verse and the principle of implication (dalalah). Understanding this proof is essential for correctly performing the second rukn of wudu, as missing either elbow invalidates the wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is washing the hands for wudu. The ruling is that washing both hands including the elbows is fard (obligatory). This is established by the Quranic verse 5:6 and consensus.
The hands here include the entire forearm up to and including the elbows.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The Quranic verse 'fa-ighsilo wujuhakum wa aydiyakum ila al-marafiq' (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:6) is the primary evidence. The imperative 'fagh-silu' indicates obligation.
The phrase 'ila al-marafiq' specifies the limit up to the elbows.
3
Analyze the textual indication for one elbow
The explicit wording (ibarat al-nass) of the verse obligates washing at least one elbow, as the addition of the plural 'marafiq' to the plural 'aydi' implies a pairing of each hand with one elbow.
This is based on the grammatical rule that juxtaposing a plural with a plural indicates a one-to-one correspondence.
4
Determine how the second elbow is included
The second elbow is obligated through dalalah (implication) and ijma (consensus). The meaning of the text implies that both elbows are equivalent, and the consensus of scholars confirms that both must be washed.
The commentator notes that after consensus, further argument is unnecessary.
5
Apply to additional body parts
Any limb or growth attached to the hand (e.g., an extra finger) must also be washed, as it is considered part of the hand. If the extra limb is above the elbow (e.g., on the shoulder), the original hand is obligatory to wash, and the extra is only recommended.
This follows from the principle that what is attached to the limb of wudu is included in the washing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It is with a kasra on the meem and fatha on the fa, and its inversion is a language, the meeting point of the arm bone and forearm. His statement: (by the wording of the text) meaning the previous speech to establish the ruling, and establishing the ruling by it is something apparent that does not require further reflection. His statement: (because the juxtaposition of the plural etc.) is a dominant rule that follows contextual clues, otherwise it would be broken by examples like 'the people wore their clothes'. His statement: (and the second elbow) if the speech were made about the entire hand it would be better, as in other texts. Commentary
This passage establishes the obligation of washing both hands including the elbows as the second rukn of wudu. The proof is twofold: the textual expression (عبارة النص) from the Quranic verse 'fa-ighsilo wujuhakum wa aydiyakum ila al-marafiq' (5:6), and the implication (دلالة) that since one elbow is mentioned, the other is included by analogy. The commentary explains that the word 'marfiq' has three pronunciations, and that extra body parts (e.g., extra finger) attached to the limb must also be washed. It also clarifies that the rule of plural-plural juxtaposition is not absolute but context-dependent.

Key Terms
المرفق (al-mirfaq): The elbow joint between the upper arm and forearm.
عبارة النص (ibarat al-nass): The explicit wording of the text that directly indicates the ruling.
دلالة (dalalah): Implication from the text, not explicit wording, but understood by common sense.

Key Takeaway
Washing both elbows in wudu is obligatory: one by explicit wording of the Quran, the other by implication and consensus.

Washing the Feet Including Ankles in Wudu
Proving the obligation of washing feet including ankles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Quranic and Hadith proof for washing feet including ankles in wudu and explain the grammatical adjacency that reconciles the alternate jar reading.

Core Idea
The Quran's command to wash feet includes the ankles; the jar reading is due to adjacency, not wiping.

This passage establishes washing the feet including the ankles as the third rukn of wudu. It draws on the Quranic verse (5:6) and a hadith, and addresses an apparent textual problem: an alternate reading with jar (kasra) that might suggest wiping. The Hanafi school clarifies that this reading is due to grammatical adjacency and does not affect the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that washing both feet including the ankles is the third pillar (rukn) of wudu. It is fard 'ayn – obligatory on every Muslim before prayer.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the Quranic verse 'wa arjulakum' (5:6) in the accusative reading, and a hadith where the Prophet ﷺ washed his feet and said 'This is wudu without which Allah does not accept prayer.'
The hadith is cited to reinforce the obligation.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
In the standard reading (nasb), 'arjulakum' is accusative, indicating washing (since washing is the action of pouring water). The alternate reading (jar) is grammatically explained as 'al-jarr bil-mujawarati' – the noun takes the case of a neighbouring word (al-ru'us) due to the conjunction waw, not because it actually indicates wiping.
This is a well-known rule in Arabic grammar: adjacency in conjunction can affect case.
4
Address the ikhtilaf
Some might argue from the jar reading that feet are to be wiped instead of washed. The Hanafi response is that this reading is due to adjacency, and the original command remains washing. Wiping is only permitted in the case of khuffs.
This refutes a potential misunderstanding.
5
Confirm the Hanafi tarjih
Both readings support washing. The feet must be washed including the ankles, as indicated by the word 'ka'bain' (dual) – each foot has two ankles, and they must be included because the limit (ghayah) 'ila' includes the limit itself.
The dual form emphasizes the obligation for both ankles of each foot.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) الركن الثالث غسل رجليه) لقوله تعالى: (وأرجلكم) ولقوله عليه السلام بعدما غسل رجليه هذا وضوء لا يقبل الله الصلاة إلا به وقراءة الجر للمجاورة مع كعبيه لدخول الغاية في المغيا والكعبان هما العظمان المرتفعان في جانبي القدم، وإشتقاقه من الارتفاع كالكعبة والكاعب التي بدأ ثديها قوله : (وقراءة الجر للمجاورة) قال ابن مالك في شرحه لكتابه المسمى بالعمدة تنفرد الواو بجواز العطف على الجواز خاصة اهـ فالأرجل مغسولة على كلتا القراءتين ولا يجوز المسح عليهما إلا في حالة التخفف وفي الكشاف إنما عطفت الأرجل على الرؤس لا لأنها تمسح، بل للتنبيه على وجوب في صب عليها لأنها تغسل بصب الماء عليها دون غيرها فكانت مظنة الإسراف وجيء بالكعبين إماطة لظن ظان إنها ممسوحة لأن المسح لم تضرب له غاية في الشرع .اهـ. قوله : (الدخول الغاية الخ) تعليل لمحذوف تقديره إنما قال : مع لدخول الغاية في المغيا في الآية المعبر فيها بإلى وحاصلة إنهما في المال واحد، وإنما ثناهما، ولم يجمعهما كالمرافق لأنه لو جمع للزم القسمة على الآحاد كالمرافق فثناهما لإفادة أن لكل رجل كعبين
(And) the third pillar: washing his feet) due to the statement of Allah Most High: 'and your feet' and due to his statement peace be upon him after washing his feet: 'This is ablution without which Allah does not accept prayer.' And the reading with jar (kasra) is for adjacency. With his ankles because the limit is included in the limited, and the ankles are the two protruding bones on the sides of the foot, and its derivation is from height like the Ka'ba and the young woman whose breasts begin to appear. His statement: (and the reading with jar is for adjacency) said ibn Malik in his commentary on his book called al-'Umdah: 'The waw alone is unique in permitting conjunction for adjacency.' So the feet are washed in both readings, and wiping over them is not permissible except in the state of wearing khuffs. And in al-Kashshaf: 'The feet are conjoined to the heads not because they are wiped, but to alert the obligation of pouring water over them, because they are washed by pouring water, so they were a likely place for excess, and the ankles were mentioned to remove the suspicion of a thinker that they are wiped, because wiping has no limit set in the Shari'ah.' His statement: (inclusion of the limit) is an explanation for an omitted phrase: He only said 'with' because the limit is included in the limited in the verse expressed with 'ila', and the result is that they are one in property, and he dualized them and did not pluralize like the elbows because if he pluralized it would necessitate division into single units like the elbows, so he dualized to indicate that each foot has two ankles.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage proves the obligation of washing both feet including the ankles as the third rukn of wudu. The proof is the Quranic verse 'wa arjulakum' (5:6) and a hadith. The commentary addresses the apparent conflict with the alternative reading 'wa arjulikum' (with jar) which might suggest wiping, but it clarifies that this is due to grammatical adjacency (al-jarr bil-mujawarah), and the feet are still washed. It distinguishes that wiping is only allowed when wearing khuffs. The dual form 'ka'bain' is used because each foot has two ankles, unlike the plural 'marafiq' which applies to many elbows.

Key Terms
الكعب (al-ka'b): The ankle bone protruding on each side of the foot.
المجاورة (al-mujawarah): Grammatical adjacency where a word takes the case of a nearby word, not the governing factor.
الغاية (al-ghayah): The limit (ila) which includes the extremity (the ankle).

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The variant reading applies only to those wearing khuffs.

Key Takeaway
The feet must be washed including the two ankle bones in wudu, as proven by the Quran and Sunnah, with the variant reading reconciled through grammatical adjacency.

Wiping a Quarter of the Head in Wudu
Proving the obligation of wiping a quarter of the head

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the obligatory amount of head wiping in wudu and explain the linguistic and customary proofs supporting the quarter.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, the minimal fard for wiping the head is one quarter, derived from the preposition 'bi' and the hand's size.

Wiping the head is a well-known pillar of wudu. But how much must be wiped? The Hanafi school uniquely holds that a quarter suffices, based on precise linguistic analysis of the Quranic verse and customary practice. This lesson unpacks that proof and clarifies the reasoning behind the quarter obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (wiping the head) and the ruling level (fard ayn, the fourth rukn of wudu). Note the specific amount: a quarter of the head.
The text states 'al-ruknu al-rabi'u mashu rub'i ra'sihi' – the fourth pillar is wiping a quarter of his head.
2
Locate the dalil
Identify the primary evidence: the Quranic verse 'wa imsahu bi ru'usikum' (5:6), specifically the preposition 'bi'.
The verse does not appear verbatim, but the commentary relies on it.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the preposition 'bi' indicates partiality: in Hanafi usul, 'bi' can denote al-ilshaq (adhesion) rather than full coverage. The passing of the hand over part of the head constitutes 'wiping' linguistically.
The hand's size (approximately a quarter of the head) provides the minimal measure.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Recognize that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and the Shafi'i school require wiping the entire head. The Hanafi tarjih favors Abu Hanifa's view.
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn. Cross-madhhab: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Understand why the quarter view is relied upon: linguistic principle (ba for partiality), customary evidence (hand size), and usul rule that a command with ba implies minimal contact.
The text says 'thumma... hadha huwa al-mu'tamad' (this is the relied-upon view).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) الركن الرابع مسح ربع رأسه الربع بضمتين، وقد تسكن الباء، والرأس أعلى كل شيء، وإنما كان الفرض الربع لأن الباء للإلصاق، واليد تقارب الربع في المقدار فإذا أمرت أدنى امرار بحيث يسمى مسحاً حصل الربع . فكان مسح الربع أدنى ما يطلق عليه اسم المسح المراد من الآية وأيضاً قد تقرر في الأصول إن الباء إذا دخلت قوله : (مسح ربع رأسه) الربع بضمتين، وقد تسكن الباء، والرأس أعلى كل شيء، وإنما كان الفرض الربع لأن الباء للإلصاق، واليد تقارب الربع في المقدار فإذا أمرت أدنى امرار بحيث يسمى مسحاً حصل الربع . فكان مسح الربع أدنى ما يطلق عليه اسم المسح المراد من الآية وأيضاً قد تقرر في الأصول إن الباء إذا دخلت
(And) the fourth pillar: wiping a quarter of his head. The quarter is with two dammas, and sometimes the ba is sukun. The head is the highest of everything. The obligation was only a quarter because the ba (preposition) is for adhesion, and the hand approximates a quarter in measure, so if you pass even the slightest passing such that it is called wiping, the quarter is achieved. So wiping a quarter was the least amount to which the name of wiping intended from the verse is applied. Also, it has been established in usul that when ba enters...
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage establishes wiping a quarter of the head as the fourth rukn of wudu. The proof from the Quran is the preposition 'bi' in 'wa imsahu bi ru'usikum' (5:6), which indicates partial contact (al-ilshaq). Since the hand typically covers about a quarter of the head when wiping, the obligation is set at that minimum. The commentary explains that the word 'rub' (quarter) is pronounced with two dammas or with sukun on the ba. The head is defined as the highest part of the body. The reasoning combines linguistic analysis and customary practice to determine the minimal obligatory amount.

Key Terms
الربع (al-rub'): One quarter of the head.
الباء (al-ba): The preposition 'bi' in the verse, interpreted as indicating partiality (al-ilshaq).
الإلصاق (al-ilshaq): Adhesion or contact; here it means the wiping action involves contact, not complete coverage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What amount of head wiping is obligatory (fard) in the Hanafi school?
A) The entire head.
B) One quarter of the head.
C) One half of the head.
D) A symbolic gesture.

Key Takeaway
The minimal fard for wiping the head in Hanafi fiqh is one quarter, based on the preposition ‘bi’ and the customary size of the hand.

Washing Both Elbows: Explicit and Implied Obligation
explaining the fard of washing both elbows with usul reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that both elbows are fard in wudu, distinguishing between the explicit text for one elbow and the implied obligation for the second, and explain the basis in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Both elbows are fard in wudu: one explicitly, the other by implication (dalalah) and consensus.

In this passage, Al-Tahtawi explains that washing the hands to the elbows is a pillar of wudu. While the Quran explicitly commands washing one elbow, the Hanafi school deduces the obligation of the second elbow through the principle of muqabilat al-jam' bi al-jam' (when a plural is paired with a plural, each individual corresponds to an individual) and consensus. This lesson clarifies that distinction and its basis in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on the first elbow
The text states that washing one of the two elbows is fard by the explicit wording (nass) of the Quranic command.
The explicit text is from Surah al-Ma'idah 5:6: 'wash your hands to the elbows'.
2
Understand the grammatical rule
The principle 'muqabilat al-jam' bi al-jam' taqtadi muqabalat al-fard bi al-fard' means when a plural (hands) is paired with a plural (elbows), each individual hand corresponds to its individual elbow. Thus, both elbows are intended.
This is a Hanafi usul principle derived from the linguistic structure of the verse.
3
Confirm the second elbow by dalalah and ijma
The second elbow is fard by implication (dalalah) because both elbows are equal, and by consensus (ijma) of the scholars.
The commentator also notes the proper pronunciation and meaning of 'mirfaq'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحد المرفقين غسله فرض بعبارة النص لأن مقابلة الجمع بالجمع تقتضي مقابلة الفرد بالفرد، والمرفق الثاني بدلالته لتساويهما وللإجماع وهو بكسر الميم، وفتح الفاء، وقلبه لغة ملتقى عظم العضد، والذراع (و) الركن الثالث غسل رجليه) لقوله تعالى: (وأرجلكم) ولقوله عليه السلام بعدما غسل رجليه هذا وضوء لا يقبل الله الصلاة إلا به وقراءة الجر للمجاورة (مع كعبيه) لدخول الغاية في المغيا والكعبان هما العظمان المرتفعان في جانبي القدم، وإشتقاقه من الارتفاع كالكعبة والكاعب التي بدأ ثديها
Washing one of the two elbows is fard by the wording of the text because the [grammatical] opposition of a plural to a plural requires mutual correspondence of individual to individual; the second elbow [is fard] by implication due to their equality and by consensus. "Elbow" is with kasra of the mim and fatha of the fa, and its inversion in language is the meeting point of the humerus and the forearm. (And) the third pillar is washing his feet due to His saying, Exalted is He: 'and your feet' and due to his saying, peace be upon him, after washing his feet: 'This is wudu with which Allah does not accept prayer except with it.' The reading with jarr is due to adjacency. 'With his heels' because the limit enters into that which is limited. The two heels are the two elevated bones on the sides of the foot, and its derivation is from elevation, like the Ka'bah and the young girl whose breast has begun to develop.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof segment from Al-Tahtawi's hashiya on Maraqi al-Falah, explaining the three pillars of wudu: washing the face, washing the hands to the elbows, and washing the feet. The main text states that washing one elbow is fard by explicit wording (nass), and the second elbow is fard by implication (dalalah) because both are equal and there is consensus (ijma). The commentator clarifies linguistic points: the word 'mرفق' (elbow) has variant pronunciations, and its root meaning is 'meeting point'. For the feet, the proof is the Quranic verse 'and your feet' (with a variant reading in the genitive, which is explained by adjacency, not altering the ruling) and a hadith that wudu is a condition for prayer acceptance. The feet must be washed including the heels; the term 'ka'bayn' is dual because each foot has two heel bones, which are prominent on the sides. The commentator also notes the derivation of 'ka'b' from elevation.

Key Terms
مرفق (mirfaq): The elbow joint, the meeting point of the upper arm and forearm; must be washed in wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the second elbow being fard in wudu according to this passage?
A) Explicit wording of the Quran
B) Analogy with the first elbow
C) Implication (dalalah) and consensus
D) Sunnah of the Prophet

Key Takeaway
Both elbows are fard in wudu: one by explicit text, the other by implication (dalalah) and consensus.

Washing the Feet: Quranic Proof and Inclusion of the Heels
explaining the proof for washing the feet and the inclusion of heels

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that washing the feet including the heels is fard in wudu, and explain the Quranic and hadith proofs as well as the interpretation of the variant reading (jarr) as adjacency.

Core Idea
Washing the feet includes the heels, supported by Quran, sunnah, and the principle that the limit (heels) enters into that which is limited (feet).

This passage continues the explanation of the third pillar of wudu: washing the feet. Al-Tahtawi cites the Quranic verse 'and your feet' and a hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) emphasizing the importance of wudu. He also addresses the variant reading with genitive ('wa arjulikum') explaining it as due to adjacency, not affecting the ruling. The commentator then clarifies that the heels must be washed, as the term 'ka'bayn' refers to the two protruding bones on each side of the foot, derived from elevation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic proof for washing the feet
The verse in Surah al-Ma'idah (5:6) commands: 'wash your feet to the heels' (in the accusative reading). The variant genitive reading is explained by adjacency to the previous word, not as a wiping command.
The Hanafi school maintains that the feet must be washed, not wiped, based on the majority reading.
2
Recognize the hadith proof
The Prophet (peace be upon him) after washing his feet said, 'This is wudu with which Allah does not accept prayer except with it.' This establishes the necessity of washing the feet for valid wudu.
The hadith is narrated by Muslim and others.
3
Understand the inclusion of the heels
The limit 'to the heels' (ila al-ka'bayn) means the heels must be washed because the limit enters into that which is limited. The heels are the two prominent bones on the sides of the foot.
The term 'ka'bayn' is dual, but each foot has two heels (inner and outer).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحد المرفقين غسله فرض بعبارة النص لأن مقابلة الجمع بالجمع تقتضي مقابلة الفرد بالفرد، والمرفق الثاني بدلالته لتساويهما وللإجماع وهو بكسر الميم، وفتح الفاء، وقلبه لغة ملتقى عظم العضد، والذراع (و) الركن الثالث غسل رجليه) لقوله تعالى: (وأرجلكم) ولقوله عليه السلام بعدما غسل رجليه هذا وضوء لا يقبل الله الصلاة إلا به وقراءة الجر للمجاورة (مع كعبيه) لدخول الغاية في المغيا والكعبان هما العظمان المرتفعان في جانبي القدم، وإشتقاقه من الارتفاع كالكعبة والكاعب التي بدأ ثديها
Washing one of the two elbows is fard by the wording of the text because the [grammatical] opposition of a plural to a plural requires mutual correspondence of individual to individual; the second elbow [is fard] by implication due to their equality and by consensus. "Elbow" is with kasra of the mim and fatha of the fa, and its inversion in language is the meeting point of the humerus and the forearm. (And) the third pillar is washing his feet due to His saying, Exalted is He: 'and your feet' and due to his saying, peace be upon him, after washing his feet: 'This is wudu with which Allah does not accept prayer except with it.' The reading with jarr is due to adjacency. 'With his heels' because the limit enters into that which is limited. The two heels are the two elevated bones on the sides of the foot, and its derivation is from elevation, like the Ka'bah and the young girl whose breast has begun to develop.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof segment from Al-Tahtawi's hashiya on Maraqi al-Falah, explaining the three pillars of wudu: washing the face, washing the hands to the elbows, and washing the feet. The main text states that washing one elbow is fard by explicit wording (nass), and the second elbow is fard by implication (dalalah) because both are equal and there is consensus (ijma). For the feet, the proof is the Quranic verse 'and your feet' (with a variant reading in the genitive, which is explained by adjacency, not altering the ruling) and a hadith that wudu is a condition for prayer acceptance. The feet must be washed including the heels; the term 'ka'bayn' is dual because each foot has two heel bones, which are prominent on the sides. The commentator also notes the derivation of 'ka'b' from elevation.

Fourth Pillar of Wudu: Wiping a Quarter of the Head
Defining the fourth pillar of wudu, its proof, extent, and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the fard amount of head wiping in wudu, derive it from the Qur'anic verse, and explain the conditions for its validity.

Core Idea
In Hanafi wudu, the fard is to wipe a quarter of the head — not the entire head, nor merely three fingers.

The fourth pillar of wudu is head wiping (masah). While the Qur'an commands 'wipe your heads', the extent of wiping has been a point of scholarly debate. This lesson examines the Hanafi position that the obligatory amount is a quarter, based on the wording of the verse and the usual coverage of the hand. We will also explore the conditions that make wiping valid and the refinement that rejects alternative views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Head wiping is a pillar (fard) of wudu. The passage states 'the fourth pillar is wiping a quarter of the head'.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is the Quranic verse: 'فامسحوا برؤوسكم' (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:6). The imperative command indicates obligation.
The verse is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalalah regarding the extent.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (inference)
The ba' in 'birusikum' indicates partiality (iltisaq) — the wiping is done with the hand, not on the entire head. When the hand is placed on the head, it covers about a quarter on average, so that is the fard amount.
This reasoning distinguishes Hanafi from Shafi'i (minimal part) and Maliki (full head).
4
Distinguish from intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Some Hanafis (al-Karkhi, al-Tahawi, al-Quduri) held that wiping the forelock (nasiyah) suffices. The commentator rejects this as 'mardud' because it is not the transmitted view and does not align with legal measurement principles.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the quarter.
5
Apply the conditions for valid wiping
Wiping must be done on the area above the ears. The wetness must be unused (not taken from another limb). However, if rain or a wet cloth contacts the head sufficiently, it counts even without hand motion.
This shows the Hanafi definition of masah focuses on transferring wetness, not hand movement per se.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) الركن الرابع مسح ربع رأسه) لمسحه ناصيته وتقدير الفرض بثلاثة أصابع مردود وإن صحح ومحل للمسح ما فوق الأذنين فيصح مسح ربعه لا ما نزل عنهما فلا يصح مسح أعلى الذوائب المشدودة على الرأس وهو لغة إمرار اليد على الشيء وشرعاً إصابة اليد المبتلة العضو ولو بعد غسل عضو لا مسحه ولا يبلل أخذ من عضو وإن أصابه ماء أو مطر قدر المفروض أجزأه (وسببه) السبب ما أفضى إلى الشيء من غير تأثير فيه (استباحة) أي إرادة فعل (ما) يكون من صلاة على المحل تعدى الفعل إلى الآلة والتقدير امسحوا أيديكم برؤسكم فيقتضي استيعاب اليد بالمسح دون الرأس واستيعاب اليد ملصقة بالرأس على ما ذكرنا لا يستغرق غالباً سوى الربع فتعين مراداً
