حتى يجب بقطعها دية كل اليد، والثلاث أكثرها، وللأكثر حكم الكل اهـ وبقيت رواية أخرى للكرخي والطحاوي واختارها القدوري، وهو مقدار الناصية. قوله: (مردود) لأنها غير المنصور رواية ودراية، أما الأول فلنقل المتقدمين رواية الربع، وأما الثاني، فلأن المسح من المقدرات الشرعية وفيها يعتبر عين ما قدر به كعدد ركعات الظهر مثلاً قوله : (ومحل المسح ما فوق (الأذنين قال في الخانية: فلو مسح على شعره إن وقع على شعر تحته رأس جاز وإن وقع على شعر تحته جبهة أو رقبة لا يجوز لأن ما على الرأس يكون من الرأس، ولهذا لو حلف لا يضع يده على رأس فلان فوضع يده على شعر تحته رأس حنث .اهـ. قوله: المشدودة على الرأس أي التي أديرت ملفوفة على الرأس بحيث لو أرخاها لكانت مسترسلة أما لو كان تحته ،رأس فلا شك في الجواز. قوله : (امرار اليد على الشيء) : أي بلطف . قوله : (إصابة اليد الخ الأولى ما ذكره غيره بقوله وشرعاً إصابة بلل لم يستعمل في غيره سواء كان المصاب عضواً، أو غيره كشعر، وخف وسيف، ونحو ذلك، وسواء كانت الإصابة باليد أو بغيرها حتى لو أصاب رأسه، أو خفه خرقة مبتلة، أو مطر، أو ثلج قدر المفروض أجزأ سواء مسحه باليد أم لا .اهـ. قوله : (ولو بعد غسل) هو ما عليه العامة، وقال الحاكم الشهيد لا يجوز المسح به أيضاً، وصححه في الإيضاح لأنه قد نص الكرخي في جامعه الكبير على الرواية عن الشيخين مفسراً معللاً فقال : إنه إذا . بفضل غسل ذراعيه لم يجز إلا بماء جديد لأنه قد تطهر به مرة وأقره في النهر وفي نوح أفندي عن المجتبى المخطون أي للحاكم مخطون .اهـ. قوله: (لامسحه يستثنى منه الأذنان فيمسحان بما بقى من بلل الرأس. قوله: (ولا) ببلل أخذ من عضو) لأنه يشترط في صحة المسح أن لا يكون البلل مستعملاً ولما أخذت البلة من العضو صارت مستعملة بالإنفصال.
And the fourth pillar is wiping a quarter of his head because he wipes his forelock. The estimation of the fard with three fingers is rejected, even if sahih. The place of wiping is the area above the ears; so wiping a quarter of it is valid, not what is below them. Wiping the top of braids tightly wound on the head is not valid. Wiping linguistically is passing the hand over something, and technically is the wet hand contacting the limb, even after washing a limb, not wiping it; and [the hand] is not moistened [by water] taken from a limb. But if water or rain hits it to the extent of the obligatory amount, it suffices. And its cause (sabab) is permissibility (istibahah), meaning the intention to perform an action, which is prayer. On the place, the verb [amr] extends to the instrument: the meaning is 'wipe your hands over your heads', which necessitates covering the hand with wiping, not the head. The hand, when attached to the head as we mentioned, usually does not cover more than a quarter, so that quarter is determined as the intended meaning from the noble verse—that is the objective. His statement: 'his forelock' refers to the front; the 'qadhal' is the back; 'fawd' (pl. afwad) are the two sides. His statement: 'estimation with three fingers' means from the smallest fingers of the hand, because fingers are the origin of the hand such that cutting them incurs the full diyah of the hand; three is the majority, and the majority has the ruling of the whole. And there remains another narration from al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi, chosen by al-Quduri, which is the amount of the forelock. It is rejected because it is not the supported view in transmission and understanding. As for the first, because the transmissions of the early scholars report a quarter; as for the second, because wiping is from the legal measurements (muqaddarat shar'iyyah), and in such matters, the precise amount is considered, like the number of rak'ahs in Zuhr. His statement: 'the place of wiping is above the ears.' Al-Khaniyyah states: if one wipes his hair that is above a head, it is permissible; if he wipes hair that is above the forehead or neck, it is not permissible, because hair that is on the head is part of the head. Thus, if someone swore not to place his hand on Zayd's head, and he placed his hand on hair that is above a head, he breaks the oath. His statement: 'tightly wound on the head' means it is wrapped and coiled on the head such that if he let it down, it would hang; but if there is a head underneath, there is no doubt about permissibility. His statement: 'passing the hand over something' means gently. His statement: 'the wet hand contacting'—the more correct is what others mentioned: technically, contacting wetness that has not been used elsewhere, whether the contacted object is a limb or something else like hair, khuff, sword, etc., and whether the contact is with the hand or not, so that if his head or khuff is hit by a wet rag, or rain, or snow to the extent of the obligatory amount, it suffices, whether he wiped with his hand or not. His statement: 'even after washing' is the common view; but al-Hakim al-Shahid said it is not permissible to wipe with it either, and this was corrected in al-Idah because al-Karkhi stated in his Jami' al-Kabir a narration from the two shaykhs explaining: 'If he [wipes] with leftover water from washing his forearms, it is not permissible except with new water because he has already purified himself with it once.' And it was confirmed in al-Nahr and in Nuh Afandi from al-Mujtaba that it is marked for al-Hakim. His statement: 'for wiping' excludes the ears; they are wiped with the leftover moisture from the head. His statement: 'nor with wetness taken from a limb' because for the validity of wiping it is required that the wetness is not used; and when the wetness is taken from a limb, it becomes used upon separation.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the fourth pillar of wudu: wiping a quarter of the head. The matn states the ruling; the hashiya clarifies the proof, extent, and conditions. The proof is the Quranic verse interpreted: 'wipe your hands over your heads' (فامسحوا بأيديكم برؤوسكم) where the ba' of 'birusikum' indicates attachment, and the command is to wipe with the hands, not the entire head. Since the hand when placed on the head typically covers about a quarter, that is the fard. The commentator rejects the view that three fingers suffice, arguing it contradicts transmitted narration (riwayah) and legal measurement (muqaddarat shar'iyyah). The place of wiping is above the ears; hair that is on the head counts as part of the head. Wiping is defined technically as the wet hand contacting a limb, but the wetness must not come from another limb (i.e., must be unused water). However, if rain or a wet cloth contacts the head sufficiently, it counts as wiping even without hand motion. The cause (sabab) of wudu is istibahah (seeking permissibility to perform prayer), meaning the intention to pray necessitates wudu.

Key Terms
مسح (masah): Technically: contacting a limb or object with wetness that has not been used elsewhere, regardless of hand motion.
ناصية (nasiyah): The front part of the head (forelock).
ربع (rub'): One quarter of the head, defined by the usual coverage of the hand when attached to the head.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimal fard amount of the head to be wiped in wudu?
A) The entire head
B) Three fingers
C) A quarter of the head
D) The forelock only

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fard for head wiping is a quarter, derived from the Qur'anic imperative and the hand's coverage; three fingers or forelock are not sufficient.

Defining Sabab and Istibahah for Wudu
Defining the cause and purpose of wudu according to Hanafi usul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the Hanafi concepts of sabab (cause) and istibahah (intended permissibility) for wudu and distinguish them from other madhahib.

Core Idea
Sabab leads without effect; istibahah is the intention to pray, not the prayer itself.

In Hanafi fiqh, the obligation of wudu is triggered by the intention to perform an act that requires purification, primarily prayer. This cause (sabab) is distinct from the effective cause ('illa) in usul al-fiqh. This lesson clarifies the precise definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defined object
Recognize that the passage defines the sabab (cause) of wudu, not wudu itself.
The sabab is what makes wudu obligatory.
2
Parse the definition of sabab
'That which leads to a thing without having an effect in it.' This means sabab is a sign or condition that precedes the act but does not directly produce it.
Contrast with 'illa (effective cause) which directly necessitates the ruling.
3
Define istibahah
Istibahah means the intention (irada) to perform an act that is prayer. Thus the sabab is the mental resolve to pray, not the physical prayer itself.
The literal meaning is 'seeking permissibility'; by custom it refers to the intention to pray.
4
Distinguish from other schools
Some schools consider the act of prayer (e.g., when its time enters) as the cause. The Hanafi view is more precise: the personal intention is the operative cause.
This affects when wudu becomes wajib: only when one intends to pray.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسببه) السبب ما أفضى إلى الشيء من غير تأثير فيه (استباحة) أي إرادة فعل (ما) يكون من صلاة
And its cause (sabab) is: that which leads to a thing without having an effect in it. (Istibahah) means the intention to perform an action that is prayer.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage defines the sabab (cause) of wudu. In Hanafi usul, sabab is 'that which leads to a thing without having an effect in it', distinguishing it from 'illa (effective cause) which has an effect. The sabab of wudu is istibahah, i.e., the desire or intention to perform an act that requires purification, such as prayer. The commentator notes this definition is by common custom (urf); the literal meaning is seeking permissibility.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): That which leads to a thing (like wudu) without having a direct effect, e.g., the intention to pray leads to wudu but does not cause it.
استباحة (istibahah): Seeking or intending to perform an act that requires purification, primarily prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The prayer itself
B) The intention to perform an act requiring purification
C) The presence of impurity
D) The time of prayer

Key Takeaway
The sabab of wudu is the intention to perform an act requiring purification, not the act itself.

The Obligatory Extent of Wiping the Head in Wudu
Defines masah and its obligatory extent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligatory amount of the head to be wiped in Hanafi wudu, explain the legal definition of masah, and distinguish the relied-upon quarter view from the rejected three-finger view.

Core Idea
Wiping a quarter of the head is the obligatory minimum; the three-finger view is rejected despite some authentication.

This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya clarifies a critical point in Hanafi wudu: exactly how much of the head must be wiped. The author defines masah linguistically and legally, affirms the quarter as the obligatory extent, and rejects the view that three fingers suffice. Understanding this ruling prevents invalid wudu and aligns practice with the relied-upon transmission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
Recognize that the main point is the minimum amount of the head that must be wiped. The text states that the quarter is the obligation and the three-finger view is 'mardud' (rejected).
2
State the relied-upon Hanafi position
The position of Abu Hanifa and the majority of the school is that the obligatory minimum is one quarter of the head. This is based on the stronger transmissions.
3
Understand the opposing intra-Hanafi view
Some Hanafi scholars like al-Karkhi, al-Tahawi, and al-Quduri transmitted that wiping three fingers is sufficient. The text dismisses this as 'mardud' even if some attempted to authenticate it.
4
Define masah linguistically and legally
Linguistically, masah is passing the hand over something. Legally, it is touching the wet hand to the limb, even if the water is from rain, provided it is fresh (not previously used). The moisture must be from a fresh source, not from another limb.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Consider someone who wipes only three fingers of the head. According to the mufta bihi position, this does not fulfill the obligation; they must wipe a quarter. If rainwater falls on the head in sufficient quantity, that counts as masah.
The text also notes that the area above the ears is the place of wiping; below the ears is not valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة رأسه) لمسحه ناصيته وتقدير الفرض بثلاثة أصابع مردود وإن صحح ومحل للمسح ما فوق الأذنين فيصح مسح ربعه لا ما نزل عنهما فلا يصح مسح أعلى الذوائب المشدودة على الرأس وهو لغة إمرار اليد على الشيء وشرعاً إصابة اليد المبتلة العضو ولو بعد غسل عضو لا مسحه ولا يبلل أخذ من عضو وإن أصابه ماء أو مطر قدر المفروض أجزأه
The book of purification. 'His head' means wiping his forelock. The estimation of the obligation with three fingers is rejected, even if authenticated. The place of wiping is what is above the ears, so wiping a quarter of it is valid, not what is below them, so wiping the top of locks tied on the head is not valid. And it (masah) linguistically is passing the hand over something, and legally is the touching of the moist hand to the limb, even if after washing a limb, not its wiping, and it is not moistened with moisture taken from a limb. And if water or rain of the obligated amount reaches him, it suffices him.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage from the commentary clarifies the Hanafi ruling on wiping the head (masah) during wudu. It begins by stating that the obligatory part is the forelock (nasiyah), and that the common view estimating the obligation as three fingers is rejected (mardud), despite some attempts to authenticate it. The correct position is that the wipe must cover at least a quarter of the head, based on the Quranic verse (5:6) and transmitted reports. The place of wiping is restricted to the area above the ears; wiping below the ears or only the locks tied above the head is insufficient. The passage then provides the linguistic and legal definitions of masah: linguistically, passing the hand over something; legally, touching the moistened hand to the limb, even if that limb has just been washed (but not with the leftover moisture from a previous wash). Importantly, the moisture must be from fresh water, not from another limb, as that would be considered used (musta'mal) and invalid. If sufficient rainwater or other pure water reaches the head without direct hand contact, it still suffices, because the essence is the moisture, not the hand's action. The Masah of the ears is a sunnah, not part of the obligatory head wipe.

Key Terms
المسح (al-masah): Linguistically, passing the hand over something. Legally (in this text), touching the moistened hand to the limb intended for purification, even if the moisture is from rain, provided it is fresh (not previously used).
الناصية (al-nasiyah): The forelock or front part of the head (the area above the forehead). In the context of masah, it is the part that must be wiped, and it is used as a standard for the quarter measure.
الربع (al-rub'): One quarter of the head. The obligatory minimum amount to be wiped in wudu according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the minimum amount of the head that must be wiped in wudu?
A) The entire head
B) Three fingers
C) One quarter of the head
D) The forelock only

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position requires wiping at least a quarter of the head above the ears; the three-finger view is rejected, and the wipe can be fulfilled by sufficient rainwater.

The Cause (Sabab) of Wudu: Definition and Legal Function
Defines the legal cause of wudu and its function

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of the sabab of wudu and distinguish it from the shart and 'illah.

Core Idea
The cause of wudu is the need to perform acts that require purity—it permits what is otherwise forbidden.

In this lesson, we examine the precise legal definition of the 'cause' (sabab) of wudu as given in Maraqi al-Falah and its commentary. The sabab is not the impurity itself, nor the act of wudu, but the intention to engage in worship that requires purification. Understanding this distinction is foundational to Hanafi fiqh of taharah and helps avoid common confusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn definition
Read the opening words: 'And its cause (sabab) is that which leads to something without having an effect in it (ghayr mu'aththir). Permission (istibahah) i.e., intending to perform an act…' This defines sabab as a non-causal antecedent.
The sabab of wudu is not impurity (hadath) but the need to perform a pure act.
2
Analyze the commentary's refinement
The sharh explains that sabab leads to something without directly effecting it, unlike 'illah which has causal power. It also clarifies that 'permission' means the desire to perform an act that requires purity (like salah).
This distinguishes sabab from 'illah: e.g., a contract (aql) is an 'illah for permissibility of marriage.
3
Apply the definition to a concrete scenario
Consider a person who intends to pray. That intention (irada) is the sabab that makes wudu obligatory. The presence of hadath alone does not trigger the ruling until the intention to pray is present.
This shows why wudu is not required when one is unconscious or asleep without intention.

إلى الشي) أي وصل إليه قوله : (من غير تأثير (فيه خرج به العلة كالعقد فإنه علة مؤثرة في حل النكاح. قوله : (أي إرادة فعل ما يكون هذا تفسير باللازم عرفاً وأصل المعنى طلب إباحة
('And its cause') The cause is that which leads to something without having an effect in it. ('permission') i.e., intending to perform an act that is from prayer... (the commentary continues on the verse, definitions, and rulings).
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage defines the 'cause' (sabab) of wudu. The matn of Maraqi al-Falah states that the cause is to permit what is otherwise not permitted (istibahah ma la yahillu illa bihi). The commentary explains that 'cause' (sabab) is a legal term: something that leads to an effect without itself being an active agent (ghayr mu'aththir). For wudu, the cause is the intention (irada) to perform acts that require purification, such as salah, touching the Quran, tawaf, etc. The sabab is like the 'reason' for the obligation, but distinct from a 'illah (effective cause) which has a causal effect. The commentary then goes into a detailed discussion of the verse (5:6) and the derivation of the quarter ruling, as well as other related masah issues. The core point is that wudu is prescribed to enable worship that would otherwise be impermissible without it. This understanding helps distinguish between the cause (sabab) and the condition (shart) of wudu; the sabab is the need for purification, while conditions are external factors like intellect and puberty.

Key Terms
السبب (al-sabab): That which leads to something else without having a direct effect on it. For wudu, the sabab is the desire or need to perform an act that requires purification (like salah).
الاستباحة (al-istibahah): Gaining permission (ibahah) to do something that was previously prohibited due to lack of purity. In the context of wudu, it means making permissible acts like salah, tawaf, and touching the mushaf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The arrival of prayer time
B) The intention to perform salah or other acts that require purity
C) The presence of ritual impurity (hadath)
D) The act of wiping the head

Key Takeaway
The sabab of wudu is the intention to perform an act that requires purity, not the impurity itself.

Removal of Obstacles: The Third Obligation of Wudu
Defining the third obligatory act of wudu and listing obstacles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish substances that form a barrier (jirm ha'il) from those that do not, and apply the Hanafi ruling on the third fard of wudu.

Core Idea
Wudu requires water to reach every part of the skin; any dense substance blocking that invalidates the wudu unless removed.

In the Hanafi school, the third obligation (fard) of wudu is not a washing action but the removal of anything that prevents water from directly contacting the skin. This lesson clarifies what counts as a barrier using examples from classical fiqh, refining common misconceptions about oily residues and dry crusts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the third fard of wudu is the removal of obstacles. This is a preparatory condition, not a washing motion.
In Hanafi fiqh, the faraid of wudu include washing the face, arms, wiping the head, and washing the feet, with removal of barriers implied in the first two.
2
Define 'jirm ha'il' (dense substance)
Understand that a barrier must be a dense solid that physically blocks water. The text specifies 'like wax and fat' as clear examples.
The term 'jirm' indicates a solid body, not a liquid or residue.
3
Apply the definition to examples
Classify each item in the passage: wax, fat, dough, clay (when dry), fish skin, dry bread, dry dirt, and eye discharge all block; oiliness (dusumah), wet dough/clay, and wet dirt do not.
The commentator explicitly distinguishes dry vs. wet states for dough and clay.
4
Distinguish blockers from non-blockers
Recognize that 'dusumah' (oil residue) does not form a barrier, but a thick layer of solid fat (shahm) does. This is the key refinement in the passage.
Common mistake: assuming any greasy substance blocks water.
5
Synthesize the purpose: water must reach skin
All three purifications (wudu, ghusl, tayammum) aim at delivering water (or earth) to the skin. The removal of obstacles ensures this goal is achieved.
The phrase 'the three revert to one' highlights the common objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة
الوضوء (و) الثالث (زوال) ما يمنع وصول الماء إلى الجسد لجرمه الحائل (كشمع وشحم) قید به لأن بقاء دسومة الزيت ونحوه لا يمنع لعدم الحائل وترجع الثلاثة لواحد هو عموم المطهر شرعاً البشرة.
قوله: (كشمع وشحم وعجين وطين وما ذكره بعضهم من عدم منع الطين، والعجين محمول على القليل الرطب ويمنع جلد السمك والخبز الممضوغ الجاف، والدرن اليابس في الأنف بخلاف الرطب قستهاني ، ويمنع الرمص، وهو ما جمد في الموق، وهو مؤخر العين أو الماق، وهو مقدمها إذا كان يبقى خارج العين بعد تغميضها قوله : (عموم المطهر شرعاً) لا يكون مطهراً إلا عند عدم حيض، ونفاس، وحدث.
Book of Purification. Wudu: And the third [obligation] is the removal of whatever prevents water from reaching the body due to its dense substance (like wax and fat). It is restricted by this because the remaining oiliness of oil etc. does not prevent due to lack of barrier. And the three [purifications] revert to one: the general purification legislatively to the skin. He said: (Like wax, fat, dough, clay. Some scholars' statement that clay and dough do not prevent is interpreted as the little wet. And fish skin and dry chewed bread prevent, and dry dirt in the nose unlike wet, is deemed light. And rheum (eye discharge) prevents, which is what solidifies in the moq (inner corner) or maq (outer corner) if it remains outside the eye after closing it. He said: (General purification legislatively) it is only purifying in the absence of menstruation, postpartum bleeding, and minor impurity.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage defines the third obligatory act of wudu: removing any physical barrier that stops water from reaching the skin. The author lists examples like wax, fat, dough, and clay. 'Dense substance' (jirm ha'il) means a solid that blocks water, unlike oil's residue which does not block. The phrase 'the three revert to one' refers to the three purifications (wudu, ghusl, tayammum) all aim at delivering water to the skin. The commentator clarifies that dough and clay are only non-blocking when wet; dry they block. Fish skin and dry bread also block. Dry dirt in the nose blocks, but wet does not. Eye discharge (ramas) blocks if it stays outside the eye after closing. The principle that water only purifies in the absence of menstrual, postpartum, and minor impurities is affirmed.

Key Terms
جرم حائل (jirm ha'il): A dense substance that physically blocks water from reaching the skin.
دسومة (dusumah): The greasy residue of oil that does not form a barrier.
رمص (ramas): Dried eye discharge that remains outside the eye after closing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is considered a barrier (jirm ha'il) that must be removed before wudu?
A) Grease from oil
B) Wet dough
C) Dry chewed bread
D) Tears on the cheek

Key Takeaway
The third fard of wudu is removing any dense solid that blocks water from the skin; non-dense residues like oil do not require separate removal.

Washing the Beard in Wudu: Thick vs. Thin and the Hanging Part
Obligation to wash outer part of thick beard and skin of light beard

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on washing thick and thin beards in wudu, applying the distinction between outer surface and skin, and identify the hanging part (mustarsil) rule.

Core Idea
For a thick beard, only the outer surface is fard; for a thin beard, water must reach the skin.

In wudu, washing the face is fard, and the beard is part of the face. But how far must water reach when the beard is thick? This passage from Maraqi al-Falah and its commentary (Hashiya al-Tahtawi) clarifies the obligation for thick vs. thin beards, addressing a disagreement within the Hanafi school. It also defines the face's boundaries: the hanging part of the beard (mustarsil) and the inner lips are not included.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on thick beard
Identify the ruling: for a thick beard (al-kaththa), the obligation is to wash only the outer surface (zahir), not the skin underneath. Note the reason: the hair takes the place of the skin.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Recognize that Abu Abdullah al-Thalji disagreed, saying the thick beard should be treated like a thin beard (obligation to reach skin). Understand his basis: the face includes the skin.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response and tarjih
The majority Hanafi view is that the thick beard's density prevents seeing the skin, so the hair functions as a substitute. The tarjih is 'fi asahh ma yufra bih' (most correct for fatwa), and earlier views (washing a third, etc.) are rejected.
4
Apply the ruling to thin beard
For a thin beard (khafifa) where the skin is visible, water must be delivered to the skin. This is the 'chosen opinion' because it is possible and maintains the facing of the face.
5
Determine the boundary of the face
The hanging part of the beard (mustarsil) beyond the face's circular boundary is not part of the face, so no obligation. Also, the inner lips when closed are part of the mouth, not the face. Inner eyes are also excluded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في تمام أحكام الوضوء ولما لم يقدم الكلام على اللحية قال (يجب) يعني يفترض (غسل ظاهر اللحية (الكثة وهي التي لا ترى بشرتها في أصح ما يفتى به) من التصاحيح في حكمها لقيامها مقام البشرة لتحول الفرض إليها ورجعوا عما قيل من الاكتفاء بثلثها أو ربعها أو مسح كلها ونحوه (ويجب) يعني يفترض إيصال الماء إلى بشرة اللحية الخفيفة) في المختار ابقاء المواجهة بها، وعدم عسر غسلها، وقيل يسقط الإنعدام كمال المواجهة بالنبات ولا يجب إيصال الماء إلى المسترسل من الشعر عن دائرة الوجه لأنه ليس منه إصالة، ولا بدلاً . عنه (ولا) يجب إيصال الماء إلى ما انكتم من الشفتين عند الإنضمام) المعتاد لأن المنضم تبع للفم في الأصح وما يظهر تبع للوجه ولا باطن العينين ولو في
قوله: (على اللحية المشهور كسر اللام وجعل صاحب الكشاف الفتح قراءة في، لا تأخذ بلحيتي. قوله : (غسل ظاهر اللحية الكثة وهي الكثيفة، وإنما زاد المصنف لفظ ظاهر إشارة إلى أنه لا يفترض غسل ما تحت الطبقة العليا من منابت الشعر. قوله: (من الإكتفاء بثلثها، أو ربعها غسلاً أو مسحاً برهان قوله: (ونحوه) من مسح ملاقي البشرة، أو عدم المسح أصلاً، وقال أبو عبد الله الثلجي : حكمها كالخفيفة. قوله: (ولا يجب إيصال الماء إلى المسترسل) أي لا يجب غسله ولا ،مسحه بلا خلاف عندنا نهر نعم سن مسحه كما في منية المصلي. قال شارحها ابن أمير حاج والذي يظهر إستنان غسله.
In the complete rulings of wudu. And since the discussion on the beard was not presented earlier, he said: (It is obligatory) meaning fard (to wash the outer part of the thick beard – the one whose skin is not visible – in the most correct fatwa position) from the corrections regarding its ruling, because it takes the place of the skin, so the obligation shifts to it. And they retracted from what was said of sufficing with washing one third or one fourth, or wiping all of it, etc. (And it is obligatory) meaning fard (to deliver water to the skin of the light beard) in the chosen opinion, to maintain the confrontation, and because washing it is not difficult. And it is said that the lack of complete confrontation is dropped due to hair growth. And it is not obligatory to deliver water to the hanging part of hair beyond the face circle, because it is not originally part of the face nor a substitute. (And) it is not obligatory to deliver water to what is hidden of the lips when closed normally, because the hidden part is part of the mouth in the most correct view, and what appears is part of the face. Nor the inner part of the eyes, even in the fingertip. He said: (Concerning the beard) the well-known is to break the lam; the author of al-Kashshaf made it fatha in reading: 'Do not take my beard.' He said: (Wash the outer part of the thick beard) meaning the dense one; the author added the word 'outer' to indicate that washing under the top layer of hair roots is not fard. He said: (Sufficing with one third or one fourth) washing or wiping is proof. He said: (And the like) includes wiping what contacts the skin, or not wiping at all. And Abu Abdullah al-Thalji said: Its ruling is like the light beard. He said: (And it is not obligatory to deliver water to the hanging part) meaning it is not obligatory to wash or wipe it without dispute among us. Yes, it is recommended to wipe it as in Munyat al-Musalli. Its commentator Ibn Amir Hajj said: What appears is that its washing is recommended.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage addresses the beard in wudu. For a thick beard (kaththa) where skin is not visible, the fard is to wash its outer surface (zahir) – the top layer – not the skin underneath. Earlier views allowing merely washing a third or fourth are rejected. For a thin beard (khafifa) where skin is visible, water must reach the skin. The hanging part of beard below the face (mustarsil) is not part of the face, so no obligation; it is recommended to wash or wipe it. The limits of the face: the inner lips (when closed) are considered part of the mouth, not the face, so not washed. Inner eyes are not washed even if a finger is inserted? The commentary notes a dispute: Abu Abdullah al-Thalji equates thick and thin beards. The relied-upon position is as stated.

Key Terms
الكثة (al-kaththa): A thick beard where the skin is not visible.
المسترسل (al-mustarsil): The part of beard hair that hangs below the face's circular boundary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the obligation for a thick beard in wudu?
A) Wash the skin under the beard
B) Wash the outer surface of the beard only
C) Wash one third of the beard
D) Wipe the beard with wet hands

Key Takeaway
For a thick beard, only washing the outer surface is fard; for a thin beard, water must reach the skin; and the hanging part (mustarsil) is not obligatory.

Rulings on Various Obstacles: Nails, Dirt, Bugs, Dye
Detailed rules on obstacles preventing water in wudu.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which obstacles (nails, dirt, flea droppings, dye) prevent water in wudu and state the Hanafi ruling for each, distinguishing between required removal and excused presence.

Core Idea
Dirt, flea droppings, and dyer's dye do not block water; only actual barriers like dough or long nails must be removed for wudu.

In wudu, water must reach every required part of the skin. However, certain common substances and conditions are excused due to hardship or because they do not actually prevent water. This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies which obstacles are problematic and which are not, based on Hanafi principles of necessity and custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Understand that any substance or condition that physically prevents water from reaching the skin must be removed before or during wudu. This is fard.
E.g., dough, wax, dry eye discharge, long nails covering the skin.
2
List the exceptions where removal is not required
From the text, note three items that do not require removal: dirt (wasakh) on the body, flea droppings (and similar small insects), and dye on a dyer's nails.
Each exception has a rationale: dirt is from the body, flea droppings are small and water penetrates, dye is excused due to necessity for dyers.
3
Understand the reasoning behind each exception
Analyze why each is not considered a barrier: dirt is generated from the body (not an external barrier), flea droppings are small and non-viscous so water passes through, dye is a hardship if removed daily for dyers.
The principle of necessity (darurah) and custom (urf) plays a role.
4
Note the intra-madhhab dispute on city dirt
Recognize that some scholars differentiated between village dirt (from dust) and city dirt (from grease), but the most correct view (fi al-ashah) and the fatwa state that both types do not prevent water.
The text uses 'fi al-ashah' and 'alayhi al-fatwa' to indicate the relied-upon position.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a description of a substance or condition, determine whether it must be removed before wudu or is excused, citing the relevant Hanafi ruling.
Test with examples like a farmer with dry mud, a baker with dough, a dyer with stained nails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة
الأظفار
الغسل للضرر ولا داخل قرحة برئت ولم ينفصل من قشرها سوى مخرج القيح للضرورة (ولو انضمت الأصابع بحيث لا يصل الماء بنفسه إلى ما بينها (أو طال الظفر فغطى ومنع وصول الماء إلى ما تحته أو كان فيه) يعني المحل المفروض غسله (ما) أي شيء (يمنع الماء) أن يصل إلى الجسد (كعجين) وشمع ورمص بخارج العين بتغميضها (وجب) أي إفترض (غسل ما تحته بعد إزالة المانع (ولا يمنع الدرن) أي وسخ وسواء للقروي، والمصري في الأصح فيصح الغسل مع وجوده (و) لا يمنع (خرء البراغيث ونحوها كونيم الذباب وصول الماء إلى البدن لنفوذ فيها لقلته وعدم لزوجته، ولا ما على ظفر الصباغ من صبغ للضرورة، وعليه الفتوى
قوله : (للضرر ) هذه العلة تنتج الحرمة وبها صرح بعضهم، وقالوا: لا يجب غسلها من كحل نجس، ولو كان أعمى، لأنه . مضر مطلقاً ولأن العين شحم، وهو لا يقبل الماء وفي ابن أمير حاج يجب إيصال الماء إلى أهداب العينين وموقيهما .اهـ. قوله : (للضرورة) ولعدم خروجه عن حكم الباطن بهذا القدر قوله : أي وسخ الأظفار وكذا دون سائر الأعضاء بالإجماع كما في الخانية، والدرر لأنه متولد عن البدن، كما في الفتح والبرهان. قوله: (في الأصح)، وعليه الفتوى وقيل : درن المدني يمنع لأنه من الودك أي الدهن، فلا ينفذ الماء منه بخلاف القروي لأن درنه من التراب والطين، فلا يمنع نفوذ الماء. قوله : (كونيم الذباب) أي زرقه . قوله: لنفوذه فيه لقلته، بل ولو منع دفعاً للحرج كما في ابن أمير حاج، ومثله في الخلاصة والبحر .
Book of Purification. Nails. Washing is for harm, and not [obligatory] inside a healed sore that healed and did not peel off except for the exit of pus due to necessity. (And if the fingers are joined such that water does not reach between them by itself, or the nail became long and covered and prevented water from reaching under it, or there is on it) meaning the place obligated to wash (something) that prevents water from reaching the body (like dough, wax, and rheum outside the eye when blinking, then it is obligated) meaning fard (to wash what is under it after removing the barrier. And dirt (wasakh) does not prevent, whether for the villager or the city dweller in the most correct view, so the wudu is valid with its existence. And (the droppings of fleas and the like like fly feces do not prevent water from reaching the body because water penetrates them due to their small size and lack of viscosity. And nor does the dye on the dyer's nail prevent due to necessity, and upon it is the fatwa. He said: (Due to harm) this cause produces prohibition, and some explicitly stated it. They said: It is not obligatory to wash it from impure kohl, even if the person is blind, because it is absolutely harmful, and because the eye is fat and does not accept water. In Ibn Amir Hajj: It is obligatory to deliver water to the eyelashes and their corners. End. He said: (Due to necessity) and because it does not exit the ruling of inner part with this amount. He said: Meaning dirt of nails, and similarly for other limbs by consensus as in al-Khaniyya and al-Durar because it is generated from the body as in al-Fath and al-Burhan. He said: (In the most correct) and upon it is the fatwa. It is said: The dirt of the city dweller prevents because it is from grease (oil), so water does not penetrate it, unlike the villager because his dirt is from dust and clay, so it does not prevent water penetration. He said: (Fly feces) meaning its excrement. He said: Because water penetrates it due to its small size, even if it did prevent, it would be excused for avoiding hardship as in Ibn Amir Hajj, and similarly in al-Khulasa and al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage covers several obstacles to water reaching skin in wudu. 1) Washing: if harm would result (e.g., from washing inside a healed wound or applying water to eye), it is not required – the ruling is based on harm (darar) which implies prohibition. For a healed sore that hasn't peeled, only the pus exit needs washing due to necessity. 2) If fingers are tightly pressed together so water cannot naturally penetrate, or if a long nail covers the skin, or if a substance like dough, wax, or dry eye discharge is present, then it is fard to remove the barrier and wash under it. 3) Dirt (wasakh) does not prevent water, whether from a villager or city dweller, according to the most correct view; the fatwa is on this. However, a minority said city dirt from grease does prevent. 4) Flea droppings and fly feces do not prevent because they are small and water penetrates them; even if they blocked, hardship would excuse. 5) The dye on a dyer's nail is excused due to necessity, and this is the fatwa. The commentary also notes that impure kohl in the eye need not be washed due to harm, though some (Ibn Amir Hajj) require washing eyelashes.

Key Terms
الدرن (al-daran): Dirt/grime on the body, considered not a barrier to water.
خرء البراغيث (khir' al-baraghith): Flea droppings, small and non-viscous, do not block water.
الصبغ (al-sabgh): Dye, especially on dyer's nails, excused due to necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A ring that prevents water
B) Flea droppings on the hand
C) Dried dough under the nail
D) Dry eye discharge on the eyelid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, only true barriers that prevent water from reaching the skin must be removed; common excusable substances like dirt, flea droppings, and dyer's dye are not considered barriers.

Taharah — Purification

Rulings on Barriers to Water in Purification
Rulings and commentary on barriers to water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which substances constitute barriers to water in wudu and ghusl, state the obligation to remove them, and recognize the exceptions and the proof for moving the tight ring.

Core Idea
A barrier preventing water must be removed; slight impurities and necessary substances are excused.

For wudu and ghusl to be valid, water must reach every required part of the body. This lesson examines what counts as a barrier (مانع) that must be removed, and what does not prevent water from reaching the skin. The text covers common substances like dirt, dye, and tight rings, providing both the general rule and exceptions based on necessity and the Prophet's practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
The text begins with 'وجب غسل ما تحته بعد إزالة المانع' (it is obligatory to wash what is under it after removing the barrier). Note the use of 'وجب' indicating obligation (wajib).
The imperative 'وجب' is used, which in Hanafi usul indicates obligation (fard) when the evidence is qat'i, but here it is used broadly for necessary actions.
2
Distinguish Between True Barriers and Non-Barriers
Substances like dough, wax, and rheum are barriers that prevent water. But dirt under nails, flea droppings, and dyer's dye do not prevent water due to water penetration or necessity. Identify the reasons given: 'لنفوذ الماء فيه لقلته' or 'للضرورة'.
The text also notes a difference between city-dweller's dirt (from fat) and villager's dirt (from dust), but the correct view is that both do not prevent.
3
Analyze the Tight Ring Ruling
The text states it is obligatory to move a tight ring, citing the hadith. This is the chosen view (al-mukhtar) despite a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad that it is not obligatory. Understand the proof and the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
The Prophet's action is used as proof. In Hanafi usul, the Prophet's act of worship (fi'l ta'abbudi) indicates obligation unless evidence shows otherwise.
4
Apply Exceptions for Harm and Post-Purification
For cracked feet, if washing causes harm, passing water over medicine suffices, and no repetition is needed. Also, after valid wudu/ghusl, cutting nails or shaving hair does not require repetition.
The principle: what has been fulfilled does not return (الساقط لا يعاد).

الفرض سقط، والساقط لا يعاد .
Washing for harm, and not inside a healed wound from which nothing separates except the exit of pus out of necessity. And if the fingers are joined such that water does not reach between them by itself, or the nail is long and covers and prevents water from reaching what is under it, or there is something that prevents water from reaching the body, like dough, wax, or rheum outside the eye due to closing it, it is obligatory to wash what is under it after removing the barrier. And dirt, that is filth, of both the villager and the city-dweller, according to the most correct view, does not prevent; the washing is valid with its presence. And it does not prevent the droppings of fleas and similar, like fly droppings, from water reaching the body because water penetrates them due to their smallness and lack of viscosity. Also, what is on the dyer's nail of dye does not prevent due to necessity, and the fatwa is upon that. And it is obligatory to move the tight ring according to the chosen of the two narrations because it apparently prevents access, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to move his ring when performing wudu. Likewise, it is obligatory to move the earring in the ear if its place is tight, and consideration is given to predominant belief of water reaching its hole, so he is not burdened to insert a stick into the hole due to hardship. 'Qurt' is what is hung from the earlobe. And if washing the cracks of his feet harms him, it is permissible to pass water over the medicine he placed in them, i.e. the cracks, out of necessity. And the ghusl is not repeated even if from janabah, nor the wiping in wudu on the place of hair after shaving it because no hadath occurs to it. Similarly, ghusl is not repeated by cutting his nail... and [I] infer from it due to no occurrence of hadath, though ghusl is recommended. Commentary: His statement 'for harm' – this cause produces prohibition, and some explicitly stated it. They said: It is not obligatory to wash it from impure kohl, even if blind, because it is harmful absolutely, and because the eye is fat and does not accept water. And in Ibn Amir Hajj, it is obligatory to deliver water to the eyelashes and their edges. End. His statement 'out of necessity' – and because it does not exit from the ruling of the inner part by this amount. His statement 'that is filth of nails' – and similarly for other than the rest of the limbs by consensus, as in al-Khaniyya and al-Durar, because it is generated from the body, as in al-Fath and al-Burhan. His statement 'according to the most correct' – and the fatwa is upon it. And it was said: the dirt of a city-dweller prevents because it is from fat, so water does not penetrate from it, unlike the villager because his dirt is from dust and clay, so it does not prevent the penetration of water. His statement 'like fly droppings' – i.e. its excrement. His statement 'because water penetrates it due to its smallness' – rather even if it prevents, out of removal of hardship, as in Ibn Amir Hajj, and similar in al-Khulasa and al-Bahr. His statement 'according to the chosen of the two narrations' – and Hasan narrated from the Imam that it is not obligatory (according to al-Khaniyya). His statement 'and likewise moving the earring in the ear is obligatory' – i.e. in ghusl. His statement 'cracks of his feet' – for example. His statement 'it is permissible to pass water over the medicine' – and if passing water over the medicine harms him, he wipes over it, and if it harms him also, he leaves it. And if none of that harms him, it is determined to the extent that does not harm him, such that if cold water harms him but not hot, and he is able to use hot, he must use hot. Then the place of permissibility of passing water over the medicine is when it does not exceed the head of the crack. If it exceeds, washing what is under the excess is determined, as in Ibn Amir Hajj, and similar in al-Durr from al-Mujtaba. But it should be qualified with the absence of harm, as is not hidden, some excellents have indicated. His statement 'because no hadath occurs' – and because the obligatory act has fallen, and what falls does not return.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage from the Hashiya of al-Tahtawi on Maraqi al-Falah deals with barriers that may prevent water from reaching the skin during wudu or ghusl. The author states the general rule: if there is any substance (like dough, wax, or rheum) that blocks water, it must be removed and the area washed. However, certain things do not count as barriers: ordinary dirt under the nails (since it is generated from the body and water penetrates it), small droppings of fleas and flies (due to their small size and lack of viscosity), and dye on the dyer's nail (due to occupational necessity, and this is the fatwa). The commentator (al-Tahtawi) explains that the dirt of a city-dweller (from fat/grease) might block water, but the correct view is that it does not, and the same applies to villagers. He also clarifies that the Prophet's practice of moving his ring during wudu provides proof that a tight ring must be moved to allow water to reach the skin. Similarly, an earring must be moved if it is tight. For cracks in the feet, if washing causes harm, one may pass water over the medicine applied, but if the medicine extends beyond the crack, the excess must be washed unless it causes harm. Importantly, once ghusl or wudu has been performed correctly, it is not repeated after shaving hair or cutting nails because no new hadath (ritual impurity) occurs; the obligation has already been fulfilled, and what falls does not return, though repeating is recommended (mustahabb).

Key Terms
درن (darun): Dirt or filth, specifically that which accumulates under the nails or on the body, which does not prevent water because it is slight and water penetrates it.
خاتم (khatim): A ring; when tight, it must be moved during wudu or ghusl to allow water to reach the skin underneath.
قرط (qurt): An earring hung from the earlobe; its hole must be wetted by moving the earring if tight, but without causing hardship by inserting a stick.
شقاق (shiqāq): Cracks or fissures in the feet; if washing them causes harm, one may pass water over the medicine applied, but the medicine must not exceed the crack.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling regarding the dirt under the nails of a villager (qarawi) when performing wudu?
A) It must be removed completely for wudu to be valid.
B) It prevents water from reaching the skin, so wudu is invalid.
C) It does not prevent water, and wudu is valid with its presence.
D) It is makruh to leave it, but wudu is valid.

Key Takeaway
Substances that prevent water must be removed, but slight impurities like dirt under nails, small droppings, and necessary dyes are excused; a tight ring must be moved based on the Prophet's practice.

Definition and Categories of Sunnah in Wudu
Definition of sunnah and categories of sunnah acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define sunnah in Hanafi terminology, distinguish between sunnah mu'akkadah and ghayr mu'akkadah, and state the legal consequence of abandoning each category.

Core Idea
Sunnah mu'akkadah carries a binding force: leaving it is sinful and risks deprivation of the Prophet's intercession.

The passage begins a new chapter on the sunan (recommended acts) of wudu. But before listing the eighteen sunnan, the author pauses to define the key term 'sunnah' itself. In Hanafi fiqh, sunnah is not a single level; it splits into mu'akkadah (emphasized) and ghayr mu'akkadah (non-emphasized). This distinction shapes the ruling: leaving a mu'akkadah sunnah is sinful, while leaving a ghayr mu'akkadah is merely leaving a merit. The passage also presents an intra-Hanafi debate on the degree of sin, with the relied-upon view that it is sinful, though less than for a wajib act.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definitions of sunnah
Identify the linguistic and terminological definitions given in the text. The terminological definition has four elements: a path in religion, by speech or action, without obligation, without censure on the one who leaves, and not a special characteristic of the Prophet.
2
Break down the terminological definition
Analyze each element: 'path' is general including sunnah and other things; 'without obligation' excludes fard; 'without censure' excludes wajib; 'not a special characteristic' excludes acts specific to the Prophet like continuous fast.
3
Distinguish mu'akkadah from ghayr mu'akkadah
Sunnah mu'akkadah is that which the Prophet consistently performed and only occasionally left; ghayr mu'akkadah is that which he did not observe with constancy. Examples of mu'akkadah: adhan, iqama, congregation, rawatib prayers, rinsing mouth and nose in wudu. Examples of ghayr mu'akkadah: wiping neck in wudu, starting with the right.
Mu'akkadah is also called sunnat al-huda; its abandonment is considered misguidance.
4
Examine the evidence for sin
The text cites the hadith 'Whoever abandons my sunnah will not attain my intercession' (Al-Talwih) and statements from Hanafi authorities (al-Qahastani, al-Jawhara, Sharh al-Manar) that leaving mu'akkadah is sinful. Understand how the threat implies sin, and that the correct view is that sin is incurred (though less than for wajib).
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), but sufficient for establishing sin in a zanni matter.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and mufta bihi
Some scholars (e.g., al-Qahastani) equate the ruling with wajib in demand; others say no sin at all. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that sin is incurred, but it is graded (tashkik) – weaker than wajib sin. Habitual abandonment may be the trigger, but the preferred view is that even a single deliberate omission is sinful.

Source Text

فصل في سنن الوضوء (يسن في حال الوضوء ثمانية عشر شيئاً) ذكر العدد تسهيلاً للطالب لا للحصر، والسنة لغة الطريقة، ولو سيئة واصطلاحاً الطريقة المسلوكة في الدين من غير لزوم على سبيل المواظبة وهي المؤكدة إن كان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم تركها أحياناً وأما التي لم يواظب عليها فهي المندوبة وإن اقترنت بوعيد لمن لم يفعلها فهي للوجوب فيسن (غسل في سنن الوضوء قوله: (ولو سيئة) منه ما وقع في حديث الطبراني من سن سنة حسنة، فله أجرها ما عمل مات بها في حياته وبعد مماته حتى تترك من من سنة سيئة، فعليه إثمها حتى تترك، ومن مرابطاً في سبيل الله جرى له أجر المرابطين حتى يبعث يوم القيامة. قوله: (واصطلاحاً الطريقة المسلوكة في الدين)، أوضح منه قول بعضهم : طريقة مسلوكة في الدين بقول، أو فعل من غير لزوم، ولا إنكار على تاركها وليست خصوصية فقولنا : طريقة الخ كالجنس يشمل السنة، وغيرها، وقولنا: من غير لزوم فصل خرج به الفرض، وبلا إنكار أخرج الواجب، وقولنا: وليست خصوصية خرج به ما هو من خصائصه وكصوم الوصال .اهـ. قوله : (على سبيل المواظبة) متعلق بقوله المسلوكة، والمراد المواظبة في غالب الأحيان، كما يفهم مما بعده. قوله : (وهي المؤكدة إن كان النبي تركها (أحياناً كالأذان والإقامة، والجماعة، والسنن الرواتب والمضمضة والإستنشاق، ويلقبونها بسنة الهدى أي أخذها هدى، وتركها ضلالة، أي أخذها من تكميل الهدى أي ،الدين ويتعلق بتركها كراهة وإساءة قال القهستاني : حكمها كالواجب في المطالبة في الدنيا إلا أن تاركه يعاقب وتاركها يعاتب اهـ. وفي الجوهرة عن القنية تاركها فاسق وجاحدها مبتدع، وفي التلويح ترك السنة المؤكدة قريب من الحرام يستحق به حرمان الشفاعة قوله : من ترك سنتي لم ينل شفاعتي وفي شرح المنار للشيخ زين الأصح أنه يأثم بترك المؤكدة لأنها في حكم الواجب، والإثم مقول بالتشكيك، فهو في الواجب أقوى منه في السنة المؤكدة اهـ وقيل : الإثم منوط بإعتياد الترك، وصحح، وقيل لا إثم أصلاً قوله: (وأما التي لم يواظب عليها كأذان المنفرد وتطويل القراءة في الصلاة فوق الواجب ومسح الرقبة في الوضوء، والتيامن وصلاة وصوم وصدقة تطوع ويلقبونها بالسنة
Chapter on the Sunan of Wudu. Eighteen things are sunnah in the state of wudu. Mentioning the number is to facilitate the student, not for limitation. Sunnah linguistically is a path, even if bad. Terminologically, it is a path followed in religion without necessity, on the basis of constancy. It is mu’akkadah (emphasized) if the Prophet (peace be upon him) sometimes left it. As for that which he did not constantly observe, it is mandubah (recommended). And if it is accompanied by a threat for the one who does not do it, then it indicates obligation. So (the text proceeds to mention the sunnan). Commentary: His statement 'even if bad' – from it is what occurred in the hadith of al-Tabarani: 'Whoever establishes a good sunnah, he has its reward and the reward of those who act upon it... and whoever establishes a bad sunnah, upon him is its sin and the sin of those who act upon it...' His statement 'terminologically, a path followed in religion' – clearer is the statement of some: 'A path followed in religion by speech or action, without obligation, and there is no censure upon the one who leaves it, and it is not a special characteristic.' So our saying 'path' etc. is like a genus that includes sunnah and other things; our saying 'without obligation' excludes fard; 'without censure' excludes wajib; 'and it is not a special characteristic' excludes things that are specific to him (the Prophet), like the continuous fast. End. His statement 'on the basis of constancy' relates to 'followed' – what is meant is constancy in most of the time, as understood from what follows. His statement 'and it is mu’akkadah if the Prophet sometimes left it' – like adhan, iqama, congregation, the regular sunnah prayers, rinsing the mouth, and sniffing water up the nose. They call this 'sunnat al-huda', i.e., adherence to them is guidance and abandoning them is misguidance, meaning adherence is part of completing guidance (i.e., religion), and abandoning them entails karahah and sin. Al-Qahastani said: 'Its ruling is like wajib in terms of demand in this world, except that the one who abandons it is punished (mu'aqab) whereas the one who abandons it (sunnah) is reprimanded (yu'atab).' End. In al-Jawhara from al-Qunyah: 'The one who abandons it is a sinner (fasiq) and the one who denies it is an innovator (mubtadi').' In al-Talwih: 'Abandoning mu’akkadah sunnah is close to haram, and one deserves deprivation of intercession.' His statement: 'Whoever abandons my sunnah will not attain my intercession.' In Sharh al-Manar by Shaykh Zayn: 'The correct view is that one commits a sin (ithm) by leaving mu’akkadah because it is in the position of wajib, and sin is said in degrees (bi al-tashkik), so it is stronger in wajib than in mu’akkadah sunnah.' End. And it was said: sin depends on habitually leaving it, and this has been corrected. And it was said: no sin at all. His statement: 'As for that which he did not constantly observe, like the adhan of an individual, lengthening the recitation in prayer beyond the required, wiping the neck in wudu, beginning with the right, and supererogatory prayer, fasting, and charity – they call it sunnah (ghayr mu’akkadah).'
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya begins a new section (fasl) on the Sunan (recommended acts) of wudu. The author first states that there are eighteen sunnan acts enumerated for ease of memorization, not to limit them. He then gives linguistic and terminological definitions of 'sunnah'. In Hanafi terminology, sunnah is a path in religion followed without obligation (luzum) and without censure for leaving it. The key distinction within sunnah is between mu’akkadah (emphasized) and ghayr mu’akkadah (non-emphasized). Mu’akkadah sunnah (also called sunnat al-huda) is that which the Prophet consistently performed and only occasionally omitted. Its abandonment is severely discouraged: some scholars equate it with wajib in terms of punishment, and it may even lead to deprivation of the Prophet's intercession. Examples include adhan, iqama, congregational prayer, the regular sunnah prayers (rawatib), and rinsing the mouth and nose in wudu. Ghayr mu’akkadah sunnah (also called sunnat al-zawa'id) consists of acts the Prophet performed but not always, such as wiping the neck in wudu or beginning with the right. These are lighter in sin. The commentary elaborates on the gradation: some say habitually leaving mu’akkadah is sinful, while others say no sin at all, but the correct view according to the text is that sin is incurred (though less than for wajib).

Key Terms
سنة (sunnah): Terminologically: a path in religion by speech or action, not obligatory, and without censure for leaving it; subdivided into mu’akkadah and ghayr mu’akkadah.
سنة مؤكدة (sunnah mu’akkadah): Emphasized sunnah: that which the Prophet consistently performed and only occasionally omitted; its abandonment is severely discouraged and considered sinful.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, sunnah mu'akkadah is a binding practice that incurs sin when abandoned, while ghayr mu'akkadah is meritorious but without sin for leaving.

The Hanafi Definition and Classification of Sunnah
Defining sunnah and its two subcategories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi technical definition of sunnah, distinguish between mu'akkadah and ghayr mu'akkadah, and explain the legal implications of each.

Core Idea
Sunnah in Hanafi fiqh is a recommended practice the Prophet sometimes left; mu'akkadah is higher than ghayr mu'akkadah but not wajib.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi technical definition of 'sunnah' as used in the context of wudu and beyond. The passage clarifies that sunnah is a path followed in religion without obligation, and it is divided into emphasized (mu'akkadah) and non-emphasized (ghayr mu'akkadah). This distinction affects the reward and blame for omission. We will break down the definition and classification with examples from the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and technical definitions
The passage gives the linguistic meaning (tariqah, a path) and the technical meaning (a religious path followed with continuity, without obligation). Note the shift from a general path to a specific religious practice.
The technical definition excludes obligatory acts (fard/wajib) by the phrase 'min ghayr luzum'.
2
Locate the key qualifying phrase
'Ala sabili al-muwazabah' (on the basis of continuity) indicates that sunnah is something the Prophet consistently did, but not so continuously as to be obligatory. 'Min ghayr luzum' explicitly negates obligation.
3
Distinguish mu'akkadah from ghayr mu'akkadah
Mu'akkadah is what the Prophet sometimes left, showing it is not obligatory but still emphasized. Ghayr mu'akkadah is that which he did not continuously practice. The criterion is the occasional omission by the Prophet.
For example, the two rak'ahs before Fajr are sunnah mu'akkadah; the regular sunnah after Zuhr (four rak'ahs) are ghayr mu'akkadah.
4
Connect to the threat clause
The passage states that if a practice is accompanied by a threat for leaving, it indicates obligation (wajib). Sunnah, even mu'akkadah, lacks such a threat. This is a key distinction between sunnah and wajib.
Hanafis consider witr and the two 'Eid prayers as wajib because of the reported threat.
5
Apply to a fiqh example
In wudu, rinsing the mouth (madmadah) and sniffing water (istinshaq) are sunnah mu'akkadah. Siwak (toothstick) is sunnah ghayr mu'akkadah. Omission of mu'akkadah is disliked, but omission of ghayr mu'akkadah is not blameworthy.
This classification helps prioritize acts of worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يسن في حال الوضوء ثمانية عشر شيئاً) ذكر العدد تسهيلاً للطالب لا للحصر، والسنة لغة الطريقة، ولو سيئة واصطلاحاً الطريقة المسلوكة في الدين من غير لزوم على سبيل المواظبة وهي المؤكدة إن كان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم تركها أحياناً وأما التي لم يواظب عليها فهي المندوبة وإن اقترنت بوعيد لمن لم يفعلها فهي للوجوب
Eighteen things are Sunnah during wudu. Mentioning the number is to facilitate the student, not for limitation. Sunnah linguistically means a path, even if evil. Technically, it is a path followed in religion without obligation, on the basis of continuity. It is mu'akkadah if the Prophet sometimes left it. As for that which he did not continuously practice, it is mandub. And if it is accompanied by a threat for one who does not do it, then it indicates obligation.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'sunnah' as used in the context of wudu. The author clarifies that the number eighteen is for ease of memorization, not exhaustive. Linguistically, sunnah means a path or way, even a bad one. Technically, in the Hanafi school, sunnah is a recommended practice that the Prophet consistently performed but sometimes omitted to show it is not obligatory. It is divided into mu'akkadah (emphasized) and ghayr mu'akkadah (non-emphasized). The mu'akkadah sunnah is one the Prophet sometimes left, like the call to prayer and congregation. The ghayr mu'akkadah is one he did not always do, like voluntary prayers. The passage also notes that if a practice is accompanied by a threat for abandonment, it becomes wajib (obligatory), but this is a separate category. The commentary from Tahtawi elaborates that the technical definition distinguishes sunnah from fard (by non-obligation) and from wajib (by the absence of blame for abandonment).

Key Terms
السنة (al-sunnah): Technically, a religious path followed with continuity, not obligatory.
المؤكدة (al-mu'akkadah): A sunnah the Prophet sometimes performed and sometimes left, indicating emphasis.
المندوبة (al-mandubah): A sunnah the Prophet did not consistently perform; recommended but not emphasized.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Sunnah of Washing Hands to Wrists in Wudu
Ruling on washing hands to wrists with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on washing hands to wrists in wudu, identify the proof, and distinguish its emphasis after sleep.

Core Idea
Washing hands to wrists is sunnah in every wudu, but emphatically so after sleep due to hadith.

In this passage, the author explains the first sunnah of wudu: washing both hands up to the wrists. While this act is recommended for all wudu, it carries special emphasis when one has just woken from sleep, based on the Prophet's command to wash before dipping into the water. We will explore the definition of the wrist as the limit, the legal ruling, and the evidence from hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled? Washing both hands to the wrists at the start of wudu. Determine the hukm level: the text says it is sunnah.
Sunnah mu'akkadah due to emphasis after sleep.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith from Muslim: 'When one of you wakes from sleep, let him not dip his hand in the vessel until he washes it.'
This is an ahad hadith, thus zanni al-thubut.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'let him not dip... until he washes' indicates the act is commanded. In Hanafi usul, al-amr yufidu al-wujub, but here the context (waking from sleep) and the reason given (he does not know where his hand spent the night) suggest the command is cautionary, making the act sunnah rather than fard.
The extension to all cases is by qiyas.
4
Apply the ruling universally
The text explicitly says this applies 'whether one woke or not', but the emphasis is stronger after sleep. The sunnah is general, but the hadith provides extra motivation for the sleeper.
Analogy from the Prophet's practice: he washed hands upon waking and also at other times.
5
Note the thamarah (practical effect)
Since it is sunnah, omitting it does not invalidate wudu, but one loses the reward and, after sleep, acts contrary to the emphatic sunnah.
The wudu is sahih; no compensation (like sajdah al-sahw) is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غسل اليدين إلى الرسغين في ابتداء الوضوء الرسغ بضم الراء وسكون السين المهملة وبالغين المعجمة المفصل الذي بين الساعد والكف وبين الساق والقدم وسواء استيقظ من نوم، أو لا ولكنه أكد في الذي استيقظ لقوله : إذا استيقظ أحدكم من منامه فلا يغمس يده في الإناء حتى يغسلها ولفظ مسلم حتى يغسلها ثلاثاً فإنه لا يدري أين باتت يده وإذا لم يمكن
Washing the hands to the wrists at the beginning of wudu. 'Rasgh' is with damma on ra', sukun on sin, and ghayn; it is the joint between the forearm and the palm, and similarly between the leg and foot. This applies whether one woke from sleep or not, but it is emphasized for the one who woke, due to his saying: 'When one of you wakes from sleep, let him not dip his hand in the vessel until he washes it.' In Muslim's wording: 'until he washes it three times, for he does not know where his hand spent the night.'
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage states the first sunnah of wudu: washing both hands up to the wrists at the beginning. The commentator defines 'rasgh' as the wrist joint, clarifying the extent of the wash. This sunnah applies in all cases, but is especially emphasized for one who has just woken from sleep, based on the hadith narrated by Muslim. The hadith commands washing the hand before dipping into the water because of potential nighttime impurity. The sunnah is established by the Prophet's practice; he washed his hands upon waking and also at other times. There is a discussion about washing both hands together or separately; the text later in the commentary suggests both are permissible, with some difference of opinion on which is better. This washing is a sunnah, not fard, but it is recommended and compensates for any missed fard in washing.

Key Terms
الرسغ (al-rasgh): The wrist joint – the area between forearm and hand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is sunnah, but more emphasized after sleep.
C) It is mustahab only for those who slept.
D) It is haram to wash hands if awake.

Key Takeaway
Washing hands to wrists at the beginning of wudu is a sunnah established by Prophetic practice, especially emphasized after sleep, and its omission does not invalidate wudu.

Defining Al-Rusgh: The Wrist and Ankle Joint in Wudu
Lexical and anatomical definition of a fiqh boundary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the anatomical definition of الرُسغ, distinguish it from adjacent joints (kaw', kursu'), and explain its significance as the boundary for washing the hands and feet in wudu.

Core Idea
The الرُسغ is the wrist and ankle joint — the precise boundary for wudu washing.

This passage defines a crucial anatomical term for the performance of wudu: the الرُسغ (rusgh). Without knowing exactly where this joint is, a person might wash less than required or exceed the proper limit. The author clarifies pronunciation, location, and distinguishes it from nearby bones. Understanding this definition ensures the wajib of washing the hands and feet is fulfilled correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its pronunciation
The term الرُسغ is given with its voweling: the ra' has a dammah, the sin has a sukun, and it is written with a ghayn (معجمة). Note the alternative pronunciation with sad instead of sin.
The author cites Allamah Qasim in Sharh al-Nuqayah for the alternative pronunciation.
2
Locate the anatomical definition
The text defines الرُسغ as the joint between the forearm (ساعد) and the hand (كف), and between the shin (ساق) and the foot (قدم). This gives two locations: the wrist and the ankle.
3
Distinguish from similar joints
The included poem differentiates three wrist bones: al-kaw' (the bone next to the thumb), al-kursu' (the bone next to the little finger), and al-rusgh (the middle). For the foot, the bone next to the big toe is called fakhdh (or Bu').
Knowing these distinctions prevents confusion in defining the exact wash boundary.
4
Apply to fiqh
In Hanafi fiqh, the hands must be washed up to the rusgh (wrist joint) and the feet up to the rusgh (ankle joint). The exact location of this joint determines whether the wudu is valid.
This is the practical legal consequence of the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرُسغ بضم الراء وسكون السين المهملة وبالغين المعجمة المفصل الذي بين الساعد والكف وبين الساق والقدم. وتضم ويقال بالصاد قاله العلامة قاسم في شرح النقاية ولقد أحسن من قال : فعظم يلي الإبهام كوع وما يلي لخنصره الكرسوع، والرسغ ما وسط وعظم يلي إيهام رجل ملقب ببوع فخذ بالعلم،
Al-Rusgh: with dammah on the ra', sukun on the sin, and with ghayn, is the joint between the forearm and the hand, and between the shin and the foot. And it is pronounced with dammah, and said with sad, as stated by Allamah Qasim in Sharh al-Nuqayah. And excellent is he who said: The bone next to the thumb is the kaw', and what is next to the little finger is the kursu', and the rusgh is the middle, and the bone next to the big toe of a man nicknamed Bu' is fakhdh, take knowledge of it.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage provides a lexical definition of the term الرُسغ (rusgh), which is a joint used in the context of wudu to define the limit of washing the hands and feet. The author clarifies its pronunciation: the ra' is vowelled with dammah, the sin is quiescent, and the ghayn is used, though it may also be pronounced with sad. The definition is both anatomical and practical: the rusgh is the joint connecting the forearm to the hand and the shin to the foot. The commentary includes a poetic line distinguishing between kaw' (thumb-side wrist bone) and kursu' (little-finger-side wrist bone), and notes a similar term for the leg. This precision is essential because the legal ruling for hand-washing extends to the rusgh, so its exact location must be known.

Key Terms
الرُسغ (al-rusgh): The joint between the forearm and hand (wrist) and between the shin and foot (ankle).

Washing Hands Before Wudu: Sunnah or Fard?
States the sunnah of washing hands and the dispute on whether it is sunnah or fard.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on washing hands at the beginning of wudu, identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether it is sunnah or fard, and apply the exception for impure hands.

Core Idea
Washing hands before wudu is sunnah, but if they are impure, it becomes fard to wash them without contaminating the water.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi explains the ruling on washing hands at the start of wudu. While the sunnah nature is clear, there is an important distinction: the ruling changes when the hands are impure. Additionally, the source of the proof (hadith) and its interpretation are examined, revealing a difference within the Hanafi school on whether this sunnah substitutes for the fard washing required for purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling and its evidence
Find the explicit statement that washing hands at the beginning of wudu is sunnah, and note the hadith evidence cited (the command to wash after waking).
The hadith is used but its implication is not taken as obligatory due to the context.
2
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Locate the different opinions: Al-Kafi says it is a sunnah that substitutes for the fard, Al-Fath says it is fard itself, Sarakhsi says it is sunnah that does not substitute. Identify the majority (mashayikh) view.
The mufta bihi is the majority view: sunnah.
3
Note the exception for impure hands
Find the condition: if the hands are impure (najis), washing them becomes fard, but care must be taken not to contaminate the water.
If water contact is unavoidable, alternative purification (tayammum) is permitted.
4
Understand the interpretation of the hadith
Analyze why the command in the hadith is not considered obligatory: it came in the context of habit (khuruj al-amr makhraj al-'adah) and is ahad, so it does not establish obligation in Hanafi usul.
The condition 'if waking from sleep' is not a restriction; the reason is not knowing where the hand has been.
5
Distinguish between the majority view and other positions
Contrast the Hanafi view with Imam Ahmad's restriction of the hadith to night sleep only. Note that the Hanafi school inclusively covers both night and day sleep.
The Hanafi approach is based on the 'illa (reason) not the specific wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غسل اليدين) على الكيفية الآتية، وأما جمعهما في غسلة واحدة كل مرة فظن صاحب المحيط أنه غير مسنون، ورده ابن أمير حاج بأنه مسنون واستدل عليه بعدة أحاديث تفيده. قال والذي تقتضيه الأحاديث أنه إذا أراد غسل اليمنى منفردةً يبدأ أولاً بصب الماء باليسرى عليها، ثم يغسل اليسرى منفردة أيضاً، أو يجمعها مع اليمنى ثانياً، وأنه إذا قصد الجمع بينهما في الغسل من غير تفريق يصب باليمنى على اليسرى، ثم يغسلهما معاً، ولا شك في جواز الكل، وأقره في البحر، وفي العيني على البخاري هل الأفضل الجمع أم التفريق خلاف بين العلماء أهـ. قوله: (في إبتداء الوضوء) تقديمه شرط في تحصيل السنة لأنهما آلة التطهير، فيبدأ بتنظيفهما كما في الإيضاح وغيره، والمراد الطاهرتان أما المتنجستان ولو قلت النجاسة فغسلهما على وجه لا ينجس الماء فرض فإن أفضى إلى ذلك تركه حتى لو لم يمكنه الاعتراف بشيء، ولو بمنديل، أو بفمه تيمم وصلى ولم يعد كما في القهستاني وغيره قال في الكافي وهذا الغسل سنة تنوب عن الفرض وقال في الفتح، بل هو فرض، وتقديمه سنة قال في البحر: وظاهر كلام المشايخ أنه المذهب وأبعد السرخسي فقال : والأصح عندي أنه سنة لا تنوب، وبه قال الشافعي قوله: (وسواء إستيقظ من نوم أولا) فإنه صح عنه عليه الصلاة والسلام إنه غسل يديه حال اليقظة قبل إدخالهما الإناء، والشرط في الحديث خرج مخرج العادة، فلا يعمل بمفهومه . قوله: (فإنه لا يدري أين باتت يده أي أوت يده فلا يختص بنوم الليل، وجعله الإمام أحمد قاصراً على نوم الليل دون نوم النهار .
(Washing the hands) in the manner that follows. As for washing both together in one wash each time, the author of Al-Muhit thought it is not sunnah, but Ibn Amir Hajj rejected this and said it is sunnah, adducing several hadiths. He said: What the hadiths require is that if one intends to wash the right hand alone, he first pours water with the left onto it, then washes the left alone, or combines it with the right a second time; and if he intends to combine both in washing without separating, he pours with the right onto the left, then washes them together. There is no doubt about the permissibility of all, and it is confirmed in Al-Bahr. In Al-Ayni on Bukhari, there is disagreement among scholars on whether combining or separating is better. His statement: (at the beginning of wudu) - giving precedence to it is a condition for obtaining the sunnah because they are the instruments of purification, so he begins cleaning them as in Al-Idah and others. What is meant is the two pure hands; as for the two impure ones, even if the impurity is little, washing them in a way that does not contaminate the water is fard. If it leads to that, he leaves it, even if he cannot scoop [with his hand], he can use a cloth or his mouth, then do tayammum and pray and not repeat, as in Al-Qahastani and others. Al-Kafi said: This washing is a sunnah that substitutes for the fard. Al-Fath said: Rather it is fard, and giving precedence to it is sunnah. Al-Bahr said: The apparent position of the mashayikh is that it is the madhhab. Sarakhsi went further and said: The correct view in my opinion is that it is sunnah that does not substitute, and al-Shafi'i also said that. His statement: (Whether he wakes up from sleep or not) - for it is authentically reported from him (peace be upon him) that he washed his hands while awake before inserting them into the vessel, and the condition in the hadith came as a habit, so its implication is not acted upon. His statement: (For he does not know where his hand spent the night) - meaning in the night, so it is not specific to night sleep. Imam Ahmad restricted it to night sleep, not day sleep.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on washing the hands at the beginning of wudu. The author explains the method: it is permissible to wash each hand separately or both together, though there is scholarly difference on which is superior. The key legal ruling is that washing the hands at the start of wudu is a sunnah (recommended), but if the hands are actually impure (najis), then it becomes fard (obligatory) to wash them in a way that does not pollute the water. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Al-Kafi considers it a sunnah that suffices for the fard, Al-Fath considers it fard itself, while the majority of mashayikh and al-Sarakhsi maintain it is a sunnah that does not replace the fard. The hadith about not immersing the hand upon waking is explained as a cautionary measure, not a specific ruling for sleep alone; the reason is that one does not know where the hand has been. Imam Ahmad restricted it to night sleep, but the Hanafi view includes both night and day sleep. The passage firmly grounds the ruling in hadith and clarifies the distinction between sunnah and fard in this context.

Key Terms
غسل اليدين (ghasl al-yadayn): Washing the hands up to the wrists (rusgh) at the beginning of wudu before rinsing the mouth and nose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Fard (obligatory)
B) Sunnah (recommended)
C) Makruh (disliked)
D) Mubah (permissible)

Key Takeaway
Washing hands at the start of wudu is sunnah muakkadah; if hands are impure, it becomes fard to wash them without contaminating the water.
